Khamr: Non-Valuable Property in the Hanafi School
Explains taqwim fall and mal status dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khamr's valuation and explain why it is considered mal but ghayr mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
Khamr is non-valuable property; its destruction does not require compensation.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: although khamr (wine) is considered property (mal) because people naturally desire and hoard it, it is not financially valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim) for Muslims. This has practical consequences: if someone destroys or usurps khamr, they are not liable for compensation, and its sale is prohibited. The ruling is based on Quranic degradation and prophetic prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine what this passage is discussing: the concept of taqwim (financial value) for khamr.
2
State the ruling
For Muslims, khamr is ghayr mutaqawwim (non-valuable).
3
List the consequences
The destroyer/usurper is not liable for compensation, and its sale is invalid.
4
Analyze the proofs
The Quran declares khamr as rijs (impure), indicating degradation; the hadith links the prohibition of drinking to the prohibition of selling.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some scholars hold khamr is not mal at all; the asahh view is that it is mal but ghayr mutaqawwim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
چھٹی بحث والسادس سقوط تقومها في حق المسلم حتى لا يضمن متلفها و غاصبها ولا يجوز بيعها لان الله تعالى لما نجسها فقد اهانها والتقوم يشعر بعزتها وقال عليه السلام ان الذى حرم شربها حرم بيعها واكل ثمنها واختلفوافی سقوط ماليتها والاصح انه مال لان الطباع تميل اليها وتضن بها ترجمہ اور چھٹی گفتگو خمر کے تقوم کا سقوط ہے مسلمان کے حق میں یہاں تک کہ اس کو ضائع کرنے والا اور اس کا غاصب ضامن نہ ہوگا اور نہ اس کی بیع جائز ہوگی اس لئے کہ جب اللہ تعالیٰ نے اسکو نا پاک قرار دیا تو اللہ نے اس کی توہین فرمائی اور تقوم اس کی عزت کی جانب مشعر ہوگا اور فرمایا نبی علیہ السلام نے کہ جس نے ثمر کے پینے کو حرام قرار دیا ہے اس نے اس کی بیچ اور اسکا من کھانے کو حرام قرار دیا ہے اور فقہاء نے اس کی مالیت کے سقوط میں اختلاف کیا ہے اور اصح یہ ہے کہ یہ مال ہے اس لئے کہ طبیعتیں اس کی جانب مائل ہوتی ہیں اور اس کے بارے میں بخل کرتی ہیں۔ تشریح یہ چھٹی بحث ہے کہ مسلمان کے حق میں خمر مال غیر متقوم ہے لہذا اگر کسی کی شراب گرادی یا غصب کر لی تو عدم تقوم کی وجہ سے عمان واجب نہ ہوگا اور اسی طرح عدم تقوم کی وجہ سے خمر کی بیع حرام ہے عدم تقوم کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اللہ نے اس کو نا پاک قرار دے کر اس کو قابل اہانت فرمایا ہے اور اس کا تقوم اس کی عزت کو ثابت کرے گا اس لئے بیع جائز نہ ہوگی۔ حدیث میں ہے کہ جسن نے خمر پینے کو حرام کیا ہے اسی ذات نے اس کی بیع کو اور اس کا شمن کھانے کو حرام کیا ہے اس سے بھی معلوم ہوا۔ کہ خمر غیر متقوم ہے یہ ہی یہ بات کہ خمر مال ہے یا نہیں اس میں فقہاء کا اختلاف ہے مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ اصبح یہ ہے کہ مال تو ہے مگر غیر متقوم ہے کیونکہ اس پر مال کی تعریف صادق آتی ہے کیونکہ مال اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی جانب طبیعتوں کا میلان ہو اور طبیعت جس کے بارے میں بخل کریں۔ خم کا عدم تقوم اس کے اتلاف کی اباحت کو ثابت نہیں کرے گا جبکہ اس کو مال مان لیا گیا ہے البتہ اگر کسی نے تلف کر دی یا غصب کی تو ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا ، ملاحظہ ہو شامی ص ۲۸۹ ج ۵
Sixth discussion: And the sixth is the fall of taqwim (valuation) for it in the right of a Muslim, such that the one who destroys it and the usurper are not liable, and its sale is not permissible, because when Allah declared it najis, He degraded it, and taqwim indicates its honor. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: The One who prohibited drinking it has prohibited its sale and consuming its price. And they differed regarding the fall of its value; the more correct (asahh) is that it is mal (property) because natures incline to it and are stingy with it. Translation: And the sixth discussion is about the fall of taqwim of khamr in the right of a Muslim, such that the one who destroys it or usurps it will not be liable for compensation, and its sale is not permissible because when Allah declared it impure, He degraded it, and taqwim indicates its honor. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: The One who prohibited its drinking has prohibited its sale and consuming its price. And the jurists differed regarding the fall of its mal status; the more correct (asahh) is that it is mal, because natures are inclined to it and are stingy with it. Explanation: This is the sixth discussion: for a Muslim, khamr is mal ghayr mutaqawwim; therefore, if someone destroys or usurps another's khamr, due to lack of taqwim, compensation is not obligatory. Similarly, due to lack of taqwim, the sale of khamr is haram. The proof for lack of taqwim is that Allah declared it impure and thus degraded it, and taqwim would indicate its honor. The hadith states that the One who prohibited drinking it also prohibited its sale and consuming its price. This also indicates that khamr is ghayr mutaqawwim. Regarding whether khamr is mal or not, the jurists differ. The author states that the more correct (asahh) view is that it is mal but ghayr mutaqawwim, because the definition of mal applies: its definition is that natures incline to it and are stingy with it. The lack of taqwim of khamr does not establish the permissibility of destroying it, as it is still considered mal; but if someone destroys or usurps it, compensation is not obligatory. See Shami, vol. 5, p. 289.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal concept of taqwim (valuation) for khamr in the Hanafi school. For Muslims, khamr is considered ghayr mutaqawwim (non-valuable), meaning it has no financial value recognized by Shari'ah. Therefore, if someone destroys another's khamr, they are not liable for compensation (damān), and if they usurp it, it is not subject to the usual guarantee. Similarly, the sale of khamr is invalid (bāṭil). The proof is that by declaring khamr rijs, Allah has degraded it, and taqwim would imply honor. A hadith confirms that the prohibition of drinking includes prohibition of selling. However, there is an ikhtilāf among jurists about whether khamr is considered māl (property) at all. The correct view according to the text is that it is māl but ghayr mutaqawwim, because humans naturally desire it and hoard it. It is important to note that lack of taqwim does not make it permissible to destroy others' property; rather, the practical effect is that no monetary compensation is required if it is damaged.

Key Terms
تقوم (taqwim): Financial valuation recognized by Shari'ah.
مال (mal): Property; something that natures incline to and hoard.
غير متقوم (ghayr mutaqawwim): Not valuable in Shari'ah; destruction does not require compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a Muslim destroys another's khamr according to this passage?
A) He must pay compensation.
B) He is not liable for compensation.
C) He must pay the full price.
D) He must buy another bottle.

Key Takeaway
For Muslims, khamr is considered property but lacks financial value; thus, it cannot be sold, and its destruction does not warrant compensation.

The Price of a Void Sale: Khamr and Debt
Ruling on price of khamr as debt payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting the price of khamr as debt payment and apply the qiyas to the sale of maytah.

Core Idea
The price of khamr is not permissible between Muslims because the sale is void.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario: a Muslim debtor offers money obtained from selling wine to repay a debt. Is it lawful for the creditor to accept? The source passage rules that it is not, drawing an analogy to dead meat. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi concept of a void sale (bay' batil) and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on the Sale of Khamr
The passage begins by stating that the sale of wine is void (batil). Recognize that in Hanafi fiqh, a batil sale does not transfer ownership, and the price is not valid consideration.
Batil is distinct from fasid: batil is void ab initio; fasid is irregular but can be rectified.
2
Apply the Ruling to Debt Payment
If a Muslim debtor pays a debt with the price obtained from selling khamr, neither the creditor may take it nor the debtor may give it. This is because the money is the price of a void sale.
The status of the money in the debtor's hand (ghasb or amanah) is a secondary ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the Qiyas to Maytah
The ruling is analogized to the sale of dead meat (maytah), which is also forbidden. The common illah (effective cause) is that both are prohibited substances (muharram al-'ayn) with no value for Muslims.
The qiyas is jaliy (clear) because the prohibition is explicit in both cases.
4
Note the Exception for Dhimmis
If the debtor is a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state), he may pay the debt with the price of khamr, as khamr is permissible for him and considered valuable.
This exception does not apply to Muslim debtors or creditors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراب کی بیع باطل ہے ومن كان له على مسلم دین فاوفاه ثمن خمر لا يحل له ان ياخذ ولا لمديون ان يؤديه لأنه ثمن بيع باطل وهو غصب في يده او امانة على حسب ما اختلفوا فيه كما في بيع الميتة ولو كان الدين على ذمى فانه يؤديه
The sale of wine is void. And if someone has a debt owed by a Muslim and that person pays him with the price of khamr, it is not permissible for the creditor to take it, nor for the debtor to pay it, because it is the price of a void sale, and it is either usurped (ghasb) in his hand or a trust (amanah) according to the different opinions, as in the sale of dead meat. If the debt is on a dhimmi, he may pay it.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion on khamr by ruling on its sale and how its price is treated in debt transactions. The sale of khamr is declared bāṭil (void). If a Muslim owes a debt to another Muslim and pays that debt with the price obtained from selling khamr (i.e., money that was given in exchange for khamr), neither the creditor may accept it nor the debtor may pay it. This is because that money is considered the price of a void sale, and thus it is either ghāṣb (usurped property) or amānah (trust) in the debtor's hand, depending on the differing views of scholars. The ruling is analogous to the sale of maytah (dead animal not slaughtered Islamically). However, if the debtor is a dhimmī (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state), he may pay the debt with such money, as khamr is permissible for him and he considers it valuable.

Key Terms
بيع باطل (bay' batil): A void sale that does not transfer ownership; the price is not valid consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The creditor must accept it as lawful payment.
B) The debtor may pay it but the creditor cannot accept.
C) Neither may take nor give it; it is the price of a void sale.
D) It is permissible if the debtor is a dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
The price of a void sale (like khamr) cannot be used to settle debts between Muslims; it is analogous to the price of dead meat.

Istihlal of Khamr Leads to Disbelief
Ruling that istihlal of khamr is kufr due to denial of qat'i text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that considering khamr halal results in kufr and explain the reason based on denial of a definitive proof.

Core Idea
Denying a qat'i prohibition makes one a disbeliever.

This lesson addresses a severe ruling: if a Muslim believes that khamr (wine) is lawful, he exits the fold of Islam. The reason is not the act of drinking itself, but the denial of a clear and certain prohibition from the Quran and Sunnah. Understanding this distinction is critical for safeguarding one's faith and correctly applying the Shariah's boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is 'istihlal' of khamr — considering it permissible. The ruling is that the person becomes a disbeliever (kafir).
This is not about drinking khamr while believing it is haram; that is a sin but not kufr.
2
Locate the Reason
The text gives the reason: 'because of his denial of the definitive proof' (dalil qat'i). The prohibition of khamr is proven by a text whose transmission and meaning are certain.
Qat'i proofs include Quranic verses like 5:90 and mutawatir hadith.
3
Understand the Epistemological Basis
In Hanafi usul, denying a qat'i ruling is equivalent to denying a necessary part of the religion (ma'lum min al-din bi'l-darurah), which removes a person from Islam.
This is distinct from denying zanni rulings, which may be sinful but not kufr.
4
Recognize the Exception
The ruling applies only to the mustahill (one who considers it halal). A person who drinks while acknowledging it is haram is a sinner but not a disbeliever.
This distinction is crucial for avoiding incorrect takfir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس انه يكفر مستحلها لانكاره الدليل القطعى ترجمہ اور پانچویں گفتگو یہ ہے کہ اس کا حلال جاننے والا کا فرٹھہرایا جائے گا اس کے انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے دلیل قطعی کا ۔ تشریح یہ پانچویں بحث ہے کہ جو خمر کو حلال سمجھے وہ کافر ہے کیونکہ وہ نص قطعی کا منکر ہے۔
And the fifth: that the one who considers it permissible is a disbeliever, due to his denial of the definitive proof. Urdu translation: 'And the fifth discussion is that the one who considers it halal will be declared a disbeliever because of his denial of the definitive proof.' Commentary: 'This is the fifth discussion: that whoever considers khamr as halal is a disbeliever, because he denies the definitive text.'
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This lesson presents the ruling that if a Muslim considers khamr (wine) to be lawful (halal), he becomes a disbeliever. The reason is that the prohibition of khamr is established by a definitive proof (dalil qat'i) from the Quran or mutawatir Sunnah. Denying a qat'i proof is tantamount to rejecting a necessary part of the religion, which takes one out of the fold of Islam. This is a severe ruling that guards against relativizing the clear prohibitions of the Shariah.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Intoxicating drink made from grapes or other substances, strictly prohibited in Islam.
مستحل (mustahill): One who considers something forbidden as lawful, specifically regarding khamr.
دليل قطعي (dalil qat'i): A definitive evidence whose transmission and meaning are certain, such as a clear Quranic verse or mutawatir hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a major sin
B) Because it involves denying a definitive proof
C) Because it leads to intoxication
D) Because it is a civil offense

Key Takeaway
Istihlal of a qat'i prohibition like khamr constitutes kufr because it rejects a certain text, not because the act itself is committed.

The Non-Valuable Status of Khamr for Muslims
Ruling on the non-valuable status of khamr for Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that khamr is not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) for Muslims, explain the two legal consequences (no liability and invalid sale), and identify the proof from the hadith and the concept of najasah.

Core Idea
Khamr has no legal value for Muslims: no compensation for destruction, and sale is void.

This lesson covers the sixth discussion from the Hidayah on khamr. The central ruling is that for Muslims, khamr lacks the status of valuable property (taqawwum). This has two key consequences: if someone destroys or usurps another's khamr, they are not liable to pay compensation, and the sale of khamr is invalid. The proof is drawn from the impure nature of khamr and a hadith linking the prohibition of drinking to the prohibition of selling. There is also an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether khamr is considered property (mal) at all, with the author declaring that it is property but non-valuable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on khamr regarding its legal value for Muslims. The hukm is that it is not mutaqawwim, meaning no liability for destruction/usurpation, and its sale is haram and batil.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: first, the impurity (najasah) declared by Allah which indicates disgrace; second, the hadith: 'Indeed, the One who made drinking it haram also made its sale and the consumption of its price haram.'
The hadith is from ahad, zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the evidence proves the ruling. The hadith links the prohibition of drinking (which is established) to the prohibition of sale and consumption of its price, showing that khamr cannot be a valid subject of sale. Similarly, najasah implies dishonor, while taqawwum implies honor, so they are incompatible.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text mentions disagreement on whether khamr retains any property value (maliat). Some say it is not mal at all due to najasah; others say it is mal (since people incline towards it) but ghayr mutaqawwim. The author declares the latter as asahh.
This ikhtilaf affects terminology but not the core ruling on liability and sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

شراب کی بیع باطل ہے
ومن كان له على مسلم دین فاوفاه ثمن خمر لا يحل له ان ياخذ ولا لمديون ان يؤديه لأنه ثمن بيع باطل وهو غصب في يده او امانة على حسب ما اختلفوا فيه كما في بيع الميتة ولو كان الدين على ذمى فانه يؤديه من ثمن الخمر والمسلم الطالب يستوفيه لان بيعها فيما بينهم جائز. ترجمہ اور جو مسلمان کہ اس کا دوسرے مسلمان پر قرض ہو پس اس نے قرض ادا کیا خمر کے شمن مسے تو مسلمان کے لئے لینا حلال نہیں اور نہ مدیون کیلئے دینا حلال ہے اس لئے کہ یہ بیع باطل کا شمن ہے اور یہ اس کے قبضہ میں یا تو غصب ہے یا امانت اس کے مطابق جو فقہاء نے اس میں اختلاف کیا ہے جیسے مردار کی بیع میں اور اگر قرض ذمی کے ذمہ ہوتو وہ قرض کو خمر کے شمن سے ادا کر سکتا ہے اور مسلمان طالب اس کو لے سکتا ہے اس لئے کہ خمر کی بیع ذمیوں کے درمیان جائز ہے۔ تشریح مردار کی بیع اور شراب کی بیع باطل ہے اور جتنی بیوع باطل ہیں ان میں بائع کے پاس جو شمن آیا ہے تو وہ ثمن بعض فقہاء کے نزدیک غصب ہے اور بعض کے نزدیک امانت ہے اور دونوں سے اپنا قرض ادا کرنا جائز نہیں ہے لہذا اگر کسی مسلمان کا کسی مسلمان پر قرض ہو اور مدیون خمر کے شمن سے قرض ادا کرے تو نہ لینا جائز ہے اور نہ دینا کیونکہ یا تو غصب ہے یا امانت ، البتہ اہل ذمہ کے لئے خمر کا معاملہ آپس میں جائز ہے لہذا اگر کسی ذمی کے اوپر کسی مسلمان کا قرض ہے اور ذمی شمن خمر سے قرض ادا کرے تو مسلمان کیلئے لینا جائز ہے کیونکہ یہ بیع باطل کا شمن نہیں ہے۔
And the sixth: the fall of its value (taqawwum) with respect to a Muslim, so that the one who destroys it or usurps it is not liable, and its sale is not permissible, because when Allah declared it impure, He disgraced it, and value implies honor. And he (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed, the One who made drinking it haram also made its sale and the consumption of its price haram.' And they differed regarding the fall of its maliat (property value), and the most correct is that it is property (mal) because natural dispositions incline towards it and they are stingy with it. Urdu translation: ... Commentary: This sixth discussion is that with respect to a Muslim, khamr is non-valuable property. So if someone's wine is spilled or usurped, due to lack of value, liability is not obligatory. Similarly, due to lack of value, the sale of khamr is haram. The proof of lack of value is that Allah declared it impure and thus disgraced it, and its value would establish its honor, so sale is not permissible. In hadith, it is mentioned... This also indicates that khamr is non-valuable. As for whether khamr is property or not, there is disagreement among jurists. The author says the most correct is that it is property but non-valuable, because the definition of property applies to it, as property is that towards which natural dispositions incline and concerning which they are miserly. Its non-valuable status does not establish the permissibility of its destruction, since it is considered property; however, if someone destroys it or usurps it, liability is not obligatory. See Shami vol. 5 p. 289. The sale of wine is void. And [the author said:] 'If someone has a debt upon a Muslim, and he pays it with the price of khamr, it is not permissible for him (the creditor) to take it, nor for the debtor to give it, because it is the price of a void sale, and it is either usurped or a trust according to what they differed about, similar to the sale of carrion. And if the debt is upon a dhimmi, he may pay it from the price of khamr, and the Muslim claimant may take it, because its sale among them (dhimmis) is permissible.' Urdu translation: ... Commentary: The sale of carrion and wine is void. And in all void sales, the price that comes to the seller is, according to some jurists, usurped, and according to others, a trust. It is not permissible to pay one's debt with either. So if a Muslim owes a debt to a Muslim and the debtor pays with the price of khamr, it is not permissible to take nor to give, because either it is usurped or a trust. However, among dhimmis, transactions in khamr are permissible. So if a Muslim has a debt upon a dhimmi, and the dhimmi pays with the price of khamr, it is permissible for the Muslim to take it, because it is not the price of a void sale (since the sale is valid among them).
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This lesson covers the sixth discussion on khamr. The central ruling is that for Muslims, khamr is not 'property of value' (mal mutaqawwim). This has two consequences: first, if someone destroys or usurps another's khamr, they are not liable to pay compensation (daman), because compensation is tied to value, and khamr has no value in Muslim hands. Second, the sale of khamr is invalid (batil) for Muslims. The proof is twofold: (1) Allah has declared khamr impure (najis), which indicates disgrace, and value implies honor, so they are incompatible; (2) a hadith states that Allah's prohibition of drinking includes prohibition of selling and consuming its price. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether khamr is considered 'property' (mal) at all. The author (al-Marghinani) declares the correct view (asahh) to be that it is property—since people naturally desire and hoard it—but it remains non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim) regarding liability and sales. The lesson then addresses a practical scenario: a Muslim creditor cannot accept payment of a debt from the price of khamr, because that price is either a usurped item or a trust in the debtor's hands, and neither can be used to discharge a debt. However, if the debtor is a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Muslim protection), the situation differs because sales of khamr among dhimmis are valid. Hence a Muslim creditor may accept such payment from a dhimmi.

Key Terms
تقوم (taqawwum): The legal status of being valuable property, such that compensation is due for its destruction and sale is valid.
مال (mal): Property; that which human nature inclines towards and which is hoarded. Here, khamr is considered mal even though non-valuable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is a Muslim not liable (daman) if he destroys another Muslim's khamr?
A) Because khamr is najis and must be destroyed
B) Because khamr is not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) for Muslims
C) Because the owner is sinful for possessing it
D) Because destruction is a form of punishment

Key Takeaway
Khamr is considered property (mal) but is not valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim) for Muslims, so no compensation is due for its destruction and its sale is invalid.

Debt Payment with the Price of Khamr: Muslim vs. Dhimmi
Ruling on debt payment with price of khamr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that a Muslim cannot pay a debt with the price of khamr, but a dhimmi can, and explain the reason based on the validity of the underlying sale.

Core Idea
A Muslim cannot pay or accept debt using khamr's price; a dhimmi can, because khamr sale among dhimmis is valid.

This lesson applies the general ruling that khamr is ghayr mutaqawwim to a specific debt scenario. The Hidayah addresses: if a Muslim owes another Muslim a debt and pays it with the price (thaman) from a khamr sale, the payment is not permissible for either party. In contrast, if the debtor is a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Muslim protection), the Muslim creditor may accept such payment because the underlying sale of khamr is valid among dhimmis. The passage also notes an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the price in a void sale is considered usurped (ghasb) or a trust (amanah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the payment
The scenario involves a debt owed by one party to another, and the debtor attempts to pay using the price (money) obtained from selling khamr. Determine the religious status of debtor and creditor: both Muslims, or one is a dhimmi.
2
Apply the ruling on sale of khamr
Recall that for Muslims, the sale of khamr is invalid (batil). Therefore, the price obtained from such a sale is not valid consideration. For dhimmis, the sale of khamr is valid among them, so their price is valid.
3
Determine the permissibility of accepting the payment
If the debtor is a Muslim, the price is from a void sale; it is considered either usurped or a trust in his hand, and thus cannot be used to discharge a debt. If the debtor is a dhimmi, the sale is valid, so the price is his lawful property, and the Muslim creditor may accept it.
The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the nature of the price (ghasb or amanah) does not affect the outcome: neither can be used to pay a debt.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسادس سقوط تقومها في حق المسلم حتى لا يضمن متلفها و غاصبها ولا يجوز بيعها لان الله تعالى لما نجسها فقد اهانها والتقوم يشعر بعزتها وقال عليه السلام ان الذى حرم شربها حرم بيعها واكل ثمنها واختلفوافی سقوط ماليتها والاصح انه مال لان الطباع تميل اليها وتضن بها ترجمہ اور چھٹی گفتگو خمر کے تقوم کا سقوط ہے مسلمان کے حق میں یہاں تک کہ اس کو ضائع کرنے والا اور اس کا غاصب ضامن نہ ہوگا اور نہ اس کی بیع جائز ہوگی اس لئے کہ جب اللہ تعالیٰ نے اسکو نا پاک قرار دیا تو اللہ نے اس کی توہین فرمائی اور تقوم اس کی عزت کی جانب مشعر ہوگا اور فرمایا نبی علیہ السلام نے کہ جس نے خمر کے پینے کو حرام قرار دیا ہے اس نے اس کی بیع اور اسکا من کھانے کو حرام قرار دیا ہے اور فقہاء نے اس کی مالیت کے سقوط میں اختلاف کیا ہے اور اصح یہ ہے کہ یہ مال ہے اس لئے کہ طبیعتیں اس کی جانب مائل ہوتی ہیں اور اس کے بارے میں بخل کرتی ہیں۔ تشریح یہ چھٹی بحث ہے کہ مسلمان کے حق میں خمر مال غیر متقوم ہے لہذا اگر کسی کی شراب گرادی یا غصب کر لی تو عدم تقوم کی وجہ سے ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا اور اسی طرح عدم تقوم کی وجہ سے خمر کی بیع حرام ہے عدم تقوم کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اللہ نے اس کو نا پاک قرار دے کر اس کو قابل اہانت فرمایا ہے اور اس کا تقوم اس کی عزت کو ثابت کرے گا اس لئے بیع جائز نہ ہوگی۔ حدیث میں ہے کہ جس نے خمر پینے کو حرام کیا ہے اسی ذات نے اس کی بیع کو اور اس کا شمن کھانے کو حرام کیا ہے اس سے بھی معلوم ہوا۔ کہ خمر غیر متقوم ہے یہ ہی یہ بات کہ خمر مال ہے یا نہیں اس میں فقہاء کا اختلاف ہے مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ اصح یہ ہے کہ مال تو ہے مگر غیر متقوم ہے کیونکہ اس پر مال کی تعریف صادق آتی ہے کیونکہ مال اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی جانب طبیعتوں کا میلان ہو اور طبیعت جس کے بارے میں بخل کریں۔ خمر کا عدم تقوم اس کے اتلاف کی اباحت کو ثابت نہیں کرے گا جبکہ اس کو مال مان لیا گیا ہے البتہ اگر کسی نے تلف کر دی یا غصب کی تو ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا، ملاحظہ ہو شامی ص ۲۸۹ ج ۵
شراب کی بیع باطل ہے
ومن كان له على مسلم دین فاوفاه ثمن خمر لا يحل له ان ياخذ ولا لمديون ان يؤديه لأنه ثمن بيع باطل وهو غصب في يده او امانة على حسب ما اختلفوا فيه كما في بيع الميتة ولو كان الدين على ذمى فانه يؤديه من ثمن الخمر والمسلم الطالب يستوفيه لان بيعها فيما بينهم جائز. ترجمہ اور جو مسلمان کہ اس کا دوسرے مسلمان پر قرض ہو پس اس نے قرض ادا کیا خمر کے شمن مسے تو مسلمان کے لئے لینا حلال نہیں اور نہ مدیون کیلئے دینا حلال ہے اس لئے کہ یہ بیع باطل کا شمن ہے اور یہ اس کے قبضہ میں یا تو غصب ہے یا امانت اس کے مطابق جو فقہاء نے اس میں اختلاف کیا ہے جیسے مردار کی بیع میں اور اگر قرض ذمی کے ذمہ ہوتو وہ قرض کو خمر کے شمن سے ادا کر سکتا ہے اور مسلمان طالب اس کو لے سکتا ہے اس لئے کہ خمر کی بیع ذمیوں کے درمیان جائز ہے۔ تشریح مردار کی بیع اور شراب کی بیع باطل ہے اور جتنی بیوع باطل ہیں ان میں بائع کے پاس جو شمن آیا ہے تو وہ ثمن بعض فقہاء کے نزدیک غصب ہے اور بعض کے نزدیک امانت ہے اور دونوں سے اپنا قرض ادا کرنا جائز نہیں ہے لہذا اگر کسی مسلمان کا کسی مسلمان پر قرض ہو اور مدیون خمر کے شمن سے قرض ادا کرے تو نہ لینا جائز ہے اور نہ دینا کیونکہ یا تو غصب ہے یا امانت ، البتہ اہل ذمہ کے لئے خمر کا معاملہ آپس میں جائز ہے لہذا اگر کسی ذمی کے اوپر کسی مسلمان کا قرض ہے اور ذمی شمن خمر سے قرض ادا کرے تو مسلمان کیلئے لینا جائز ہے کیونکہ یہ بیع باطل کا شمن نہیں ہے۔
And the sixth: the fall of its value (taqawwum) with respect to a Muslim, so that the one who destroys it or usurps it is not liable, and its sale is not permissible, because when Allah declared it impure, He disgraced it, and value implies honor. And he (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed, the One who made drinking it haram also made its sale and the consumption of its price haram.' And they differed regarding the fall of its maliat (property value), and the most correct is that it is property (mal) because natural dispositions incline towards it and they are stingy with it. Urdu translation: ... Commentary: This sixth discussion is that with respect to a Muslim, khamr is non-valuable property. So if someone's wine is spilled or usurped, due to lack of value, liability is not obligatory. Similarly, due to lack of value, the sale of khamr is haram. The proof of lack of value is that Allah declared it impure and thus disgraced it, and its value would establish its honor, so sale is not permissible. In hadith, it is mentioned... This also indicates that khamr is non-valuable. As for whether khamr is property or not, there is disagreement among jurists. The author says the most correct is that it is property but non-valuable, because the definition of property applies to it, as property is that towards which natural dispositions incline and concerning which they are miserly. Its non-valuable status does not establish the permissibility of its destruction, since it is considered property; however, if someone destroys it or usurps it, liability is not obligatory. See Shami vol. 5 p. 289. The sale of wine is void. And [the author said:] 'If someone has a debt upon a Muslim, and he pays it with the price of khamr, it is not permissible for him (the creditor) to take it, nor for the debtor to give it, because it is the price of a void sale, and it is either usurped or a trust according to what they differed about, similar to the sale of carrion. And if the debt is upon a dhimmi, he may pay it from the price of khamr, and the Muslim claimant may take it, because its sale among them (dhimmis) is permissible.' Urdu translation: ... Commentary: The sale of carrion and wine is void. And in all void sales, the price that comes to the seller is, according to some jurists, usurped, and according to others, a trust. It is not permissible to pay one's debt with either. So if a Muslim owes a debt to a Muslim and the debtor pays with the price of khamr, it is not permissible to take nor to give, because either it is usurped or a trust. However, among dhimmis, transactions in khamr are permissible. So if a Muslim has a debt upon a dhimmi, and the dhimmi pays with the price of khamr, it is permissible for the Muslim to take it, because it is not the price of a void sale (since the sale is valid among them).
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This lesson covers the sixth discussion on khamr. The central ruling is that for Muslims, khamr is not 'property of value' (mal mutaqawwim). This has two consequences: first, if someone destroys or usurps another's khamr, they are not liable to pay compensation (daman), because compensation is tied to value, and khamr has no value in Muslim hands. Second, the sale of khamr is invalid (batil) for Muslims. The proof is twofold: (1) Allah has declared khamr impure (najis), which indicates disgrace, and value implies honor, so they are incompatible; (2) a hadith states that Allah's prohibition of drinking includes prohibition of selling and consuming its price. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether khamr is considered 'property' (mal) at all. The author (al-Marghinani) declares the correct view (asahh) to be that it is property—since people naturally desire and hoard it—but it remains non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim) regarding liability and sales. The lesson then addresses a practical scenario: a Muslim creditor cannot accept payment of a debt from the price of khamr, because that price is either a usurped item or a trust in the debtor's hands, and neither can be used to discharge a debt. However, if the debtor is a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Muslim protection), the situation differs because sales of khamr among dhimmis are valid. Hence a Muslim creditor may accept such payment from a dhimmi.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): Price; the money paid in exchange for a sale. In this context, the price obtained from selling khamr.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state who is protected by the state and pays jizyah. Dhimmis are allowed to practice their religion and engage in commerce that may be prohibited for Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, why can a Muslim creditor accept payment from a dhimmi debtor who uses the price of khamr?
A) Because dhimmis are not required to follow Islamic law
B) Because the sale of khamr among dhimmis is valid, so the price is lawful
C) Because the creditor has no choice
D) Because the dhimmi has repented

Key Takeaway
A Muslim cannot pay or accept debt with the price of khamr, but a dhimmi can pay a Muslim creditor with such money because khamr sale among dhimmis is valid.

The Prohibition of Benefiting from Khamr (Intifa')
Ruling that any form of benefit from khamr is prohibited, with a critique of the reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that any form of benefit from khamr is prohibited and evaluate the reasoning given in the text.

Core Idea
Benefiting from khamr is prohibited in all forms, even non-consumptive uses.

This lesson examines the seventh discussion in the text: the absolute prohibition of deriving any benefit from khamr. The author provides two reasons, but the commentary points out a weakness in one of them by citing the example of dung, which is also impure yet permissible. We will unpack the ruling and its supporting logic to understand the precise basis of the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Scope
Read the passage and identify the core legal ruling: that intifa' (any benefit) from khamr is prohibited. The text lists explicit examples: feeding to animals, medicine, enema, pouring into a hole.
The ruling extends beyond drinking to all forms of utility.
2
Analyze the First Reason: Benefit from Najis is Haram
The author states that benefiting from impurity (najis) is prohibited. This is a general principle. Assess whether this reason alone is sufficient.
Hanafi fiqh allows benefit from some najis items, e.g., dung for fertilizer.
3
Analyze the Second Reason: Obligation to Avoid Khamr
The second reason: khamr is wajib al-ijtinab (obligatory to avoid), and benefiting from it involves 'approach' (qurb), contradicting avoidance. This seems stronger.
This reason emphasizes the specific prohibition of khamr itself.
4
Examine the Critique: The Dung Counterexample
The commentary notes that the second reason 'breaks' with dung: dung is also najis, yet it is permissible to benefit from it. This shows the second reason is flawed if applied generally. The real basis must be the specific textual prohibition of khamr.
Thus, the prohibition is not simply due to najasah, but due to the specific ruling of khamr.
5
Conclude the Mufta Bihi Ruling and Its Basis
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that any form of benefit from khamr is haram. The strongest basis is the specific injunction to avoid khamr entirely, not the general principle of impurity.
This distinction matters when comparing khamr to other najis items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسابع حرمة الانتفاع بها لان الانتفاع بالنجس حرام ولانه واجب الاجتناب وفي الانتفاع به اقتراب ترجمہ اور ساتویں گفتگو خمر سے انتفاع کا حرام ہونا ہے اور اسلئے کہ یہ واجب الاجتناب ہے اور اس سے نفع اٹھانے میں اس سے نزدیکی ہے۔ تشریح یہ ساتویں گفتگو ہے کہ ثمر سے نفع اٹھانا حرام ہے یعنی جانوروں کو پلانا دوا دارد کرنا حقنہ لین یا ذ کر کے سوراخ میں ڈالنا سب حرام ہیں کیونکہ خمر سے دوری ضروری ہے اور اس سے انتفاع میں اس سے قرب ہے، مگریہ تعلیل گوبر سے ٹوٹ جاتی ہے۔
And the seventh: the prohibition of benefiting from it, because benefiting from impurity is prohibited, and because it is obligatory to avoid it, and in benefiting from it there is approach (to it). Urdu translation: 'And the seventh discussion is the prohibition of benefiting from khamr, and because it is obligatory to avoid, and in benefiting there is approach.' Commentary: This is the seventh discussion: that benefiting from khamr is prohibited. For example, feeding it to animals, using it as medicine, taking an enema with it, or pouring it into a hole—all are prohibited, because keeping away from khamr is necessary, and benefiting from it involves approaching it. However, this reasoning is broken by dung (which is also najis but permissible to benefit from).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This lesson covers the seventh discussion: the prohibition of deriving any benefit (intifa') from khamr. The text gives two reasons: first, benefiting from impurities is generally prohibited; second, since Muslims must avoid khamr completely, any act of benefiting from it would involve closeness to it, contradicting that avoidance. The commentary lists examples such as feeding it to animals, using it as medicine, or taking an enema with it. However, the commentator notes a flaw in the second reason: the same reasoning would apply to dung (gobar), which is also impure but is permissible to benefit from (as fertilizer). So the stronger basis is the specific prohibition of khamr, not just its impurity.

Key Terms
انتفاع (intifa'): Taking any form of benefit or utility from something, beyond just consumption.
واجب الاجتناب (wajib al-ijtinab): That which it is obligatory to avoid completely; a category for things like khamr.
قرب (qurb): Approach or proximity; used here to denote that benefiting brings one close to the forbidden substance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Using it in food seasoning
B) Feeding it to animals
C) Using it as perfume
D) Using it for cleaning

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of benefiting from khamr is based on its specific prohibition in the Shari'ah, not just its impurity, as seen by the contrast with dung.

Hadd Punishment for Drinking Khamr
Ruling that the drinker of khamr receives hadd regardless of intoxication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of hadd for drinking khamr, explain why intoxication is not a condition for its application, and identify the evidence (dalil) and its abrogation.

Core Idea
Drinking khamr incurs hadd regardless of intoxication, based on prophetic command and ijma.

This lesson examines the eighth discussion in the chapter of hudud: the fixed punishment for drinking khamr. The Hanafi school rules that the hadd (flogging) applies solely due to the act of drinking, not because of intoxication. The primary evidence is a hadith in which the Prophet commanded flogging for the drinker, with a graduated penalty that originally included execution for repeated offenses. However, the execution part was abrogated, leaving flogging as the established punishment, and the Companions unanimously agreed on this. The exact number of lashes is detailed elsewhere in the chapter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is drinking khamr (wine). The ruling is that the drinker must receive the hadd punishment (flogging). This applies regardless of whether the person becomes intoxicated.
The passage explicitly states 'وان لم يسكر' — even if he does not become intoxicated.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith: 'Whoever drinks khamr, flog him; if he returns, flog him; if he returns, flog him; if he returns, kill him.'
The hadith is a hadith ahad (solitary), with zanni thubut and zanni dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative verb 'فاجلدوه' (flog him) indicates obligation (wujub). The command is general, covering every drinker of khamr regardless of intoxication. The repetition shows gradation for repeat offenders.
The Hanafi principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
4
Note the abrogation
The part ordering execution ('فاقتلوه') has been abrogated (mansukh). Thus, only flogging remains. This abrogation is established by the consensus (ijma) of the Companions.
The passage says 'الا ان حكم القتل قد انتسخ'.
5
Reference the measure of punishment
The passage states that the specific number of lashes is covered in the chapter on hudud. The Hanafi school holds it to be 80 lashes for a free person and 40 for a slave, based on the practice of Caliph Umar.
This lesson focuses on the application of hadd, not the exact number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثامن ان يحد شاربها وان لم يسكر منها لقوله عليه السلام من شرب الخمر فاجلدوه فان عاد فاجلدوه فان عاد فاجلدوه فان عاد فاقتلوه الا ان حكم القتل قد انتسخ فبقى الجلد مشروعا وعليه انعقد اجماع صحابة رضى وتقديره ما ذكرناه في الحدود ترجمہ اور آٹھویں گفتگو یہ ہے کہ اس کے پینے والے کو حد لگائی جائے گی اگر چہ اس سے نشہ نہ ہو نبی علیہ السلام کے فرمان کی وجہ سے کہ جس نے خمر پی پس اس کو کوڑے لگاؤ پس اگر دوبارہ پیئے پھر کوڑے لگاؤ پھر اگر لوٹے پھر کوڑے لگا ؤاگر پھر بپئے تو اس کو قتل کر دو مگر یہ کہ قتل کا حکم منسوخ ہو گیا ہے تو کوڑے لگانا مشروع بن کر باقی رہ گیا اور اس پر اجماع صحابہ منعقد ہو گیا ہے اور حد کی مقدار وہ ہے جس کو ہم حد کے بیان میں ذکر کر چکے ہیں۔ تشریح یہ مواضع عشرہ میں آٹھویں جگہ ہے کہ نمر کے پینے والے پر حد جاری کی جائے گی جیسا کہ اس حدیث میں آیا ہے جو گذری
And the eighth: that its drinker is punished with hadd, even if he does not become intoxicated from it, due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'Whoever drinks khamr, flog him; if he returns, flog him; if he returns, flog him; if he returns, kill him.' However, the ruling of killing has been abrogated, so flogging remains legislated, and upon this the consensus of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) has been established. And its measure is what we have mentioned in the chapter of hudud. Urdu translation: ... Commentary: This is the eighth place among the ten topics, that the drinker of khamr will be subjected to hadd, as stated in the hadith that has passed.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This lesson presents the eighth discussion: the legal penalty (hadd) for drinking khamr. The proof is a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered flogging for the drinker, with repeated floggings for repeat offenses, and even execution for persistence. However, the execution penalty was later abrogated, leaving only flogging. The Companions agreed (ijma) on the legitimacy of flogging. Notably, the hadd applies even if the person does not become intoxicated, because the act of drinking itself is the offense. The specific number of lashes is not given here but is detailed in the chapter on hudud (penalties).

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed, unchangeable punishment prescribed by the Shariah for specific crimes, here flogging for drinking khamr.
جلد (jald): Flogging; the physical punishment of whipping with lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It was abrogated (mansukh)
C) It applies only to non-Muslims
D) It was misinterpreted

Key Takeaway
The hadd punishment for drinking khamr is applied based on the act of drinking alone, not on intoxication, as established by prophetic command and consensus of the Companions.

Nabidh: Shareek's Permissibility View and Hanafi Refutation
Imam Muhammad presents Shareek's view and refutes it with ijma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on intoxicating nabidh and evaluate Shareek bin Abdullah's proof using the consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
Ijma of the Sahaba overrides a zanni Quranic interpretation—nabidh is haram.

This passage presents a key dispute: Shareek bin Abdullah argues that nabidh (date-drink) is permissible based on a Quranic verse. The Hanafi author refutes him by invoking the consensus of the Companions. Understanding how ijma functions as a decisive proof is essential for grasping Hanafi methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on intoxicating nabidh. The school holds it is haram, even in small amounts.
2
Identify Shareek's view and his proof
Shareek bin Abdullah claims nabidh is mubah (permissible), citing Quran 16:67, where Allah mentions taking intoxicant and good provision from fruits, interpreting it as a favor.
Shareek was a successor and one of Abu Hanifa's followers, but his view on nabidh was rejected by the mainstream.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
The Hanafi author responds by interpreting the verse as referring to the early period of Islam before prohibition, or as a rebuke. The consensus of the Companions is the primary proof.
The response uses ta'wil (interpretation) to neutralize the apparent meaning of the verse.
4
Analyze the role of ijma
Explain why ijma of the Sahaba is considered stronger than a zanni (probable) interpretation of a Quranic verse. In Hanafi usul, ijma is a qat'i proof.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: Hanafis consider any amount of intoxicating nabidh haram; Shareek's view would permit it if not drunk to intoxication.
The thamarah is critical for fatwa and daily practice.

كتاب الاشربة
وقال شریک بن عبد الله انه مباح لقوله تعالى تتخذون منه سكرا ورزقا حسنا امتن علينا به وهو بالمحرم لا يتحقق ولنا اجماع الصحابة رضى الله عنهم ويدل عليه ما رويناه من قبل والاية محمولة على الابتداء وكانت الاشربة مباحة كلها وقيل اراد به التوبيخ معناه والله اعلم تتخذون منه سكرا ورزقا حسنا .
ترجمہ اور شریک بن عبد اللہ نے کہا کہ یہ مباح ہے اللہ تعالیٰ کے فرمان تتخذون منه سكراً ورزقا حسنا کی وجہ سے اللہ نے اس کی وجہ سے ہم پر احسان جتایا اور امتنان حرام میں متحقق نہیں ہوتا اور ہماری دلیل صحابہ کا اجماع ہے اور اس کے اوپر وہ حدیث دال ہے جس کو ہم پہلے روایت کر چکے ہیں اور آیت ابتداء اسلام پر محمول ہے اور (جب ) تمام قسم کی شرا میں مباح تھیں اور کہا گیا ہے کہ اس سے توبیخ مراد ہے جس کے معنی (واللہ اعلم) یہ ہیں کہ تم ان سے سکر بناتے ہو اور رزق حسن چھوڑتے ہو۔ تشریح شریک بن عبد الله اتمی ولادت 95 ھ وفات کے لیے امام صاحب کے تلاندہ میں سے ہیں یہ سکر کو مباح قرار دیتے ہیں اور ان کا مذکورہ آیت سے استدلال ہے جیسا کہ واضح ہے مگر ہماری دلیل ان کے خلاف، ا صحابہ کا اجماع ہے - ہم قبل میں جو حدیث پیش کر چکے ہیں یعنی الخمومن ھاتین.... الخ اس سے بھی سیکٹر کی حرمت ثابت ہے۔ ابھی یہ آیت تو یہ ابتداء اسلام پر محمول ہے اور اس وقت سب شرابین مباح تھیں اور بعض حضرات نے کہا ہے کہ آیت سے مراد تو ریخ ہے کہ اللہ کی ان نعمتوں کو ٹھیک طرح استعمال کرنے کے بجائے تم اس سے سکر بناتے ہوا د ر اس کے رزق حسن کو چھوڑ دیتے ہو۔ تنبیہ ۔ یہ آیت کی ہے شراب مکہ میں حرام نہیں ہوئی تھی ہجرت کے بعد حرام ہوئی تاہم اس کی آیت میں بھی مسکرا کے بعد ورزقاً حسن فرما کر متنبہ فرمادیا کہ جو چیز آئندہ حرام ہونے والی ہے اس پر رزق حسن کا اطلاق موزوں نہیں، بہر حال یہ جواب عمدہ ہے نہ یہ کہ اس کو تو بیخ پر محمول کیا جائے۔
The Book of Drinks. And Shareek bin Abdullah said it is permissible because of Allah's statement: 'You take from it intoxicant and good provision' – He favored us with it, and favor is not actualized with what is prohibited. Our proof is the consensus of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and the hadith we narrated before supports it. The verse is interpreted to refer to the beginning (of Islam) when all drinks were permissible. It is said that it means rebuke: 'You take intoxicant from it and leave the good provision.' Translation: Shareek bin Abdullah said it is permissible due to the verse... our proof is consensus... the verse is interpreted as referring to the beginning of Islam... Commentary: Shareek bin Abdullah was a follower of Imam Abu Hanifa... he considers nabidh permissible using the verse... but our proof against him is the consensus of the Companions... and the hadith we presented earlier proves its prohibition. The verse is interpreted as referring to the beginning of Islam when all drinks were permissible. Some said it is a rebuke: you make intoxicant from it and leave the good provision. Note: This verse was revealed when wine was not yet prohibited in Mecca; it was prohibited after Hijrah. By mentioning 'good provision' after 'intoxicant', it alerts that what is to be prohibited later cannot be called good provision. This response is excellent, not that it should be interpreted as rebuke.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about the permissibility of nabidh (date-drink). Shareek bin Abdullah argues it is permissible based on Quran 16:67, which mentions taking intoxicant (sakr) and good provision (rizq hasan) from fruits, and he claims that Allah's favor indicates permissibility. The Hanafi author responds that the consensus (ijma) of the Companions is the decisive proof against Shareek. The verse is interpreted as referring to the early period of Islam when all intoxicating drinks were mubah (permissible) before prohibition. Alternatively, it is a rebuke: instead of using Allah's blessings properly, you make intoxicants. Thus the verse does not indicate permissibility of nabidh after prohibition. The note cautions that even in that verse, mentioning 'good provision' after 'intoxicant' hints that the intoxicant will not be considered good provision later, so the verse does not contradict the prohibition.

Key Terms
نبیذ (nabīdh): A drink made from dates or raisins soaked in water; its intoxicating form is considered haram in Hanafi school.
سكر (sukr): Intoxicating drink made from dates; used in the verse to mean intoxicant.
اجماع الصحابة (ijmāʿ aṣ-ṣaḥābah): Consensus of the Companions of the Prophet, considered a binding proof in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits intoxicating nabidh based on the consensus of the Companions, interpreting the Quranic verse as referring to a temporary period or as a rebuke.

Naqi' al-Zabib and the Distinction Between the Four Drinks and Khamr
Ruling on raisin infusion and comparative analysis of four drink types vs khamr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on naqi' al-zabib and explain the five practical differences between the prohibition of the four drinks and khamr.

Core Idea
Not all intoxicants are equal: the four drinks are ijtihadi haram, khamr is qat'i haram.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses the legal status of raisin infusion (naqi' al-zabib) and then provides a comparative analysis between the four types of intoxicating drinks (nabidh, naqi', etc.) and khamr (wine). The author clarifies that while all are haram, there are significant differences in the strength of the prohibition, the consequence of considering them halal, the conditions for hadd, and the nature of their impurity. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correct application of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating that naqi' al-zabib (raisin infusion) is haram when it becomes strong (ferments) and bubbles. This is the same ruling as for date infusion (naqi' al-tamr). Note that al-Awza'i considered it permissible, but the Hanafi position follows the Companions' consensus.
The key condition for haram is that it becomes intoxicating.
2
Understand the ijtihadi nature of the prohibition
The prohibition of these four drinks is ijtihadi (derived through reasoning), not qat'i (definitively proven from clear texts). This means that someone who considers them halal is not declared a disbeliever, unlike the case with khamr.
Ijtihadi rulings are open to scholarly disagreement without takfir.
3
Compare the five differences between these drinks and khamr
The text enumerates five differences: (1) Strength of prohibition: ijtihadi vs qat'i. (2) Takfir: one who considers these halal is not a disbeliever; one who considers khamr halal is. (3) Hadd: hadd is obligatory only upon intoxication for these drinks, but for khamr even a single drop triggers hadd. (4) Najasah: these drinks have two narrations (light or heavy impurity), while khamr's impurity is heavy by consensus. (5) [Implicit] The evidence for khamr is clear from the Quran, while for these drinks it is from hadith and ijtihad.
These differences have practical implications in fiqh and fatwa.
4
Apply the distinctions in practice
For example, if someone drinks a drop of khamr, hadd is obligatory immediately. But if someone drinks a drop of naqi' al-zabib without becoming intoxicated, no hadd is applied. Similarly, if someone denies the prohibition of khamr, they may be deemed a disbeliever; but denying the prohibition of naqi' al-zabib does not lead to takfir because it is an ijtihadi ruling.
These nuances reflect the Hanafi school's careful approach to criminal and purity laws.

And as for naqi' al-zabib (raisin infusion), it is haram when it becomes strong and ferments. The difference of al-Awza'i appears in it, and we have explained the meaning before. Translation: And as for naqi' al-zabib, which is the raw water of raisins, it is haram when it becomes strong and boils. Al-Awza'i differs in it, and we have given the reason before. Commentary: Naqi' al-zabib is the raw water of raisins and currants; when it ferments and becomes strong, it is haram. Al-Awza'i considers it permissible, and the same response we gave for naqi' al-tamr applies here. Summary: All these types except nabidh are haram. The difference in the prohibition of the four drinks: However, the prohibition of these drinks is less than the prohibition of khamr, so much so that one who considers them halal does not become a disbeliever, while one who considers khamr halal becomes a disbeliever. Because their prohibition is ijtihadi (based on reasoning) while the prohibition of khamr is qat'i (definitive). Hadd (fixed punishment) is not obligatory for drinking them unless intoxication occurs, but for khamr, hadd is obligatory for even a drop. Their najasah (impurity) is light in one narration and heavy in another, while the najasah of khamr is heavy in a single narration. Translation: ... Commentary: The four mentioned types of wine are haram, but there are differences between khamr and other wines. 1. Khamr's prohibition is strong, others light. 2. One who considers khamr halal is a disbeliever, others not. 3. Khamr's prohibition is qat'i from Quran, others are zanni. 4. Hadd is obligatory for one drop of khamr, for others only when intoxicated. 5. Khamr has one narration (heavy impurity), others have two. These five differences we have listed separately for ease.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings. First, the ruling on naqi' al-zabib (raisin infusion): it is haram when it becomes intoxicating (ferments and bubbles). Al-Awza'i differs, considering it permissible. The Hanafi response is the same as for naqi' al-tamr (date infusion), i.e., the consensus of the Companions and the hadith. The author concludes that all these types (except nabidh? Actually the summary says 'all these except nabidh' but earlier nabidh was also considered haram, so perhaps it means except the earlier discussed nabidh? Or a textual issue; the context indicates all four are haram. Second, a comparative analysis of the four types (which include nabidh, naqi', etc.) versus khamr. Five differences are enumerated: (1) The prohibition of these drinks is ijtihadi (derived through reasoning), hence less strong, whereas khamr's prohibition is qat'i (definitive from the Quran). (2) One who considers these halal is not deemed a disbeliever, but one who considers khamr halal is. (3) Hadd (fixed punishment) is only obligatory for these drinks if intoxication occurs, but for khamr even a single drop triggers hadd. (4) The impurity of these drinks is light in one narration and heavy in another, while khamr's impurity is heavy by consensus (one narration). (5) These are rulings specific to Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
نقيع الزبيب (naqīʿ al-zabīb): The raw water of raisins; considered haram when it becomes intoxicating.
اجتهادي (ijtihādī): A ruling derived through ijtihad (scholarly reasoning), not definitively proven from primary sources.
قطعيّة (qaṭʿiyyah): A ruling that is definitively proven from clear Quranic or mutawatir hadith texts.

Key Takeaway
The four intoxicating drinks are haram, but their prohibition is ijtihadi, so the rulings on takfir, hadd, and najasah differ from those of khamr.

Selling and Compensation for Non-Khamr Intoxicants: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale and tort liability for non-khamr intoxicants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa considers non-khamr intoxicating drinks as mal mutaqawwim and state the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between his view and that of the Sahibayn regarding sale and liability.

Core Idea
Property value of non-khamr intoxicants remains because no definitive proof strips it – unlike khamr.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents an internal Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. At issue is whether non-khamr intoxicating drinks (e.g., nabidh made from dates or raisins) qualify as shari'ah-recognized property (mal mutaqawwim). Abu Hanifa affirms their property status, allowing valid sale and compensation for destruction; the Sahibayn reject it. Understanding the basis of their disagreement reveals the Hanafi method of weighing definitive vs. probable evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed rulings
Note that two rulings are in dispute: the validity of sale and liability for destruction. Three positions exist: Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn (both), and a special narration from Abu Yusuf regarding sale after cooking.
2
Locate the key concept: mal mutaqawwim
The passage states these drinks are mal mutaqawwim because no qat'i (definitive) evidence removes their property value. Understand the Hanafi definition: property that has lawful uses (e.g., conversion to vinegar).
Compare with Shafi'i position that only halal commodities are property.
3
Contrast with khamr
Khamr is excluded because definitive evidence strips its property value. The reason: khamr has absolutely no permissible use; any transformation (vinegar) creates a new substance. Non-khamr drinks can be lawfully transformed into vinegar.
The four types of non-khamr intoxicants (e.g., date wine, raisin wine) are not inherently najis like khamr.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Abu Hanifa: sale valid, destroyer pays value (qimah), not the like (mithl). Sahibayn: sale invalid, no liability. Note also Abu Yusuf's narration allowing sale if cooking reduces more than half but less than two-thirds.
The practical consequence is clear for trade and insurance claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز بيعها ويضمن متلفها عند ابي حنيفة خلافا لهما فيهما لانه مال متقوم وما شهدت دلالة قطعية بسقوط تقومها بخلاف الخمر غيران عنده تجب قيمتها لامثلها على ما عرف ولا ينتفع بها بوجه من الوجوه لانها محرمة وعن ابى يوسف انه يجوز بيعها اذا كان الذاهب بالطبخ اكثر من النصف دون الثلثين
ترجمہ اور دیگر شرابوں کی بیع جائز سے اور تلف کرنے والا ضامن ہوگا ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اختلاف ہے صاحبین کا ان دونوں میں بیع وضمان میں ) اس لئے کہ یہ (غیر خمر ) مال متقوم ہے اور کوئی دلیل قطعی ان کے تقدم کے سقوط پر شاہد نہیں ہے بخلاف خمر کے علاوہ اس بات کے کہ ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ان شرابوں کی قیمت واجب ہوگی نہ کہ ان کا مثل اس تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو پہچان لیا گیا ہے اور ان شرابوں سے کسی بھی طرح فائدہ اٹھانا جائز نہیں کیونکہ وہ محرم ہیں اور ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے کہ ان کی بیع جائز ہے جب پکا کر جو حصہ جاتا رہے وہ نصف سے زیادہ ہو دو تہائی سے کم ہو۔
And their sale is permissible and the one who destroys them is liable according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Sahibayn) regarding both matters, because they are mutaqawwim property (valuable in shari'ah), and no definitive evidence has witnessed the fall of their property value, unlike khamr. However, according to him, their value (qimah) is obligatory, not their like (mithl), as is known. And no benefit may be taken from them in any way because they are haram. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that their sale is permissible if what is lost by cooking is more than half but less than two-thirds. Translation: And the sale of other wines is permissible and the one who destroys them is liable according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because these are mutaqawwim property and there is no definitive proof that their property value has fallen, unlike khamr. However, according to Abu Hanifa, their value (not their like) is obligatory. And no benefit may be taken from them because they are haram. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that their sale is permissible if what is lost by cooking is more than half but less than two-thirds.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding non-khamr intoxicating drinks (the four types). Abu Hanifa holds that their sale is valid because they are considered 'mal mutaqawwim' (property recognized by shari'ah) – they have value because they can be used for purposes other than intoxication, such as making vinegar. There is no definitive (qat'i) evidence that they lose all property value like khamr does. Therefore, if someone destroys another's possession of these drinks, they must pay compensation (value, not the like). However, using them for any purpose is still prohibited because they are haram. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree and consider their sale invalid and the destroyer not liable, because they analogize them to khamr which has no property value. Additionally, a narration from Abu Yusuf allows sale if the reduction by cooking (i.e., converting to vinegar) reduces more than half but less than two-thirds of the liquid. This is a concession for business purposes. The passage highlights the Hanafi principle that property value remains until definitive proof removes it.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Property that is legally recognized and has value in shari'ah, making transactions valid and destruction compensable.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or compensation for destroying someone's property; here, the destroyer must pay the value (qimah).
مثل (mithl): The like (exact equivalent) of an item; Hanafis usually require mithl for fungible goods, but here value is required for these drinks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone destroys another's container of intoxicating nabidh, what is the destroyer required to pay?
A) The like (mithl) of the nabidh
B) The value (qimah) of the nabidh
C) Nothing because it is haram
D) Double the value as penalty

Key Takeaway
Non-khamr intoxicants retain property value until definitive proof strips it, making sale valid and destruction compensable.

Sale and Guarantee of Non-Khamr Intoxicants: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale/guarantee of non-khamr drinks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on the sale and guarantee of non-khamr intoxicating drinks and explain the difference from Sahibayn's view, citing the concepts of mal mutaqawwim and dalalah qat'iyyah.

Core Idea
A drink is mal mutaqawwim unless definitive evidence drops its valuation; Abu Hanifa extends this to non-khamr intoxicants.

This passage presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute over the legal treatment of intoxicating drinks other than khamr. While both Abu Hanifa and his two disciples agree on the prohibition of consumption, they differ on whether these drinks can be sold and whether their destruction triggers financial liability. The crux lies in the concept of mal mutaqawwim and the role of definitive evidence (dalalah qat'iyyah).

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify that according to Abu Hanifa, the sale of non-khamr intoxicating drinks is valid, and if someone destroys them, he must pay their value (qimah). The drinks are considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim).
2
Identify Sahibayn's opposing position
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the sale is invalid and no guarantee is due, because they do not consider these drinks as mal mutaqawwim due to prohibition and lack of permissible benefit.
3
Follow the reasoning of each side
Abu Hanifa reasons that no definitive evidence (dalalah qat'iyyah) exists to negate the valuation of these drinks, so they remain mutaqawwim. Sahibayn argue that since the drinks are prohibited and cannot be used beneficially, they are not mutaqawwim.
Compare with khamr, whose valuation is dropped by definitive text.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response to Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa acknowledges that these drinks are haram (no benefit allowed), but maintains that prohibition alone does not remove their status as valuable property; only a definitive text can do that.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and Abu Yusuf's conditional view
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, sale is valid and guarantee is due; under Sahibayn, sale is void. Additionally, Abu Yusuf permits sale if the drink is cooked until more than half (but less than two-thirds) evaporates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز بيعها ويضمن متلفها عند ابي حنيفة خلافا لهما فيهما لانه مال متقوم وما شهدت دلالة قطعية بسقوط تقومها بخلاف الخمر غيران عنده تجب قيمتها لامثلها على ما عرف ولا ينتفع بها بوجه من الوجوه لانها محرمة وعن ابى يوسف انه يجوز بيعها اذا كان الذاهب بالطبخ اكثر من النصف دون الثلثين
And it is permissible to sell them, and one who destroys them is liable for their value according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in both matters, because they are valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), and no definitive evidence (dalalah qat'iyyah) has been witnessed to negate their valuation, unlike khamr. Additionally, according to him, their value (qimah) is owed, not their like (mithl), as is known. And no benefit can be taken from them in any way because they are prohibited. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that it is permissible to sell them when the amount lost by cooking is more than half but less than two-thirds.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the sale and guarantee of the four intoxicating drinks (ashribah arba'ah). Abu Hanifa holds that their sale is valid and that if someone destroys them, he must pay their value (qimah, not mithl). His reasoning is that these drinks are mal mutaqawwim (valuable property in Shar'iah) because there is no definitive evidence (dalalah qat'iyyah) that negates their valuation, unlike khamr whose valuation is dropped by definitive text. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) disagree: for them, sale is invalid and no guarantee is due because these drinks are not considered valuable property since they are prohibited and cannot be used beneficially. However, Abu Yusuf has a separate narration that if the drink is cooked until more than half but less than two-thirds evaporates, its sale is permissible. Even according to Abu Hanifa, although sale is valid, no benefit (intifa') from these drinks is allowed because they are haram. The guarantee is for their value, not a like-for-like replacement, as established in the madhhab.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and can be sold or compensated; its use is not prohibited by definitive text.
دلالة قطعية (dalalah qat'iyyah): Definitive evidence (from Quran or mutawatir) that establishes a ruling conclusively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Abu Hanifa's ruling that non-khamr intoxicating drinks are mal mutaqawwim?
A) These drinks are not intoxicating at all.
B) There is no definitive evidence (dalalah qat'iyyah) that drops their valuation.
C) The Sahibayn agree with him on this point.
D) These drinks can be used for medicinal purposes.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa permits the sale of non-khamr intoxicants because no definitive evidence negates their status as valuable property, though the Sahibayn differ.

Sale and Compensation of Non-Khamr Intoxicants: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Explains difference in sale validity and liability for khamr and other intoxicants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the sale of non-khamr intoxicants, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A intoxicant that is not grape-wine (khamr) can be validly sold and compensated, according to Abu Hanifa, but the school follows the Sahibayn.

This passage addresses an important intra-Hanafi dispute: whether intoxicating drinks other than khamr (grape wine) can be sold validly and whether destroying them incurs compensation. While Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the prohibition of use and the validity of transaction, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that all intoxicants are alike in being non-valuable property. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for correctly applying rulings on transactions and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, recognize that Abu Hanifa holds the sale of non-khamr intoxicants is valid and compensation is due, while the Sahibayn hold it is invalid and no compensation. Note the common ground: both agree all intoxicants are haram to consume.
2
Analyze the reasoning (dalil) for each position
Abu Hanifa argues that these drinks are mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) because no definitive proof (qat'i dalil) has nullified their value, unlike khamr. The Sahibayn counter that since benefiting is haram, they cannot be considered valuable; value requires permissibility of use.
The key concept is the definition of mal mutaqawwim.
3
Note the additional nuance from Abu Yusuf
A narration from Abu Yusuf allows sale only if the drink has reduced by between half and two-thirds (through cooking). This shows a variation within the Sahibayn's position.
This is an exception within the general prohibition.
4
Recognize the common ground
Both sides agree that all intoxicants are haram and that a Muslim cannot benefit from them. The disagreement is only about transactional and liability consequences.
The dispute is over legal effects, not permissibility of use.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the reason for tarjih
Later authorities (e.g., Al-Hidayah) give preference to the Sahibayn's view as more precautionary and consistent with the general prohibition. Thus, in fatwa, the sale of non-khamr intoxicants is invalid and no compensation is due.
The authority weight of the Sahibayn's view is from the zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح چھٹا فرق یہ ہے کہ خمر کی بیع باطل ہے اور دیگر شرابوں کی بیع جائز ہے (۷) خمر کے مختلف پر ضمان نہیں ہے دیگر شرابوں کے لف پر ضمان ہے مگر یہ امام صاحب کا مذہب ہے صاحبین کے نزدیک ان کی بھی بیع جائز نہیں اور ان کے مختلف پر بھی ضمان واجب نہیں ہے لیکن امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ دیگر شراب مال متقوم ہیں اور کسی دلیل قطعی سے یہ ثابت نہ ہوسکا کہ یہ مال متقوم نہیں ہے بخلاف خمر کے کہ اس کا تقوم ساقط ہے اس وجہ سے اس کی بیع باطل ہے اور جو اس کو تلف کر دے اس پر ضمان نہیں ہے لیکن مسلمان حرام میں تصرف نہیں کر سکتا اس لئے جب ضمان واجب ہوگا تو مثل کا نہ ہوگا بلکہ شرابوں کی قیمت کا ضمان واجب ہوگا۔
البتہ اس حکم میں دونوں کا اشتراک ہے کہ جیسے خمر سے انتفاع جائز نہیں ہے اسی طرح دیگر شرابوں سے بھی ہر قسم کا انتفاع حرام ہے کیونکہ یہ سب بھی حرام ہیں امام ابو یوسف سے منقول ہے کہ خمر کے علاوہ دیگر شرابوں کی بیع اسی وقت جائز ہوگی جبکہ وہ دو ثلث سے کم اور نصف سے زیادہ ختم ہوگئی ہو ۔
تنبیہ صاحب نتائج الافکار نے یہاں یہ اعتراض کیا ہے کہ جب یہ سب حرام ہیں اور ان سے کسی طرح کا انتفاع جائز نہیں تو پھر یہ قیمتی کیسے ہو سکتی ہیں جبکہ علماء نے تصریح کردی کہ مال متقوم ہونے کے یہی معنی ہیں کہ اس سے انتفاع شرعا مباح ہو تو جب انتفاع مباح نہیں تو تقوم بھی ندارد ہو گا علاوہ بریں دلیل قطعی اعتقاد کے واسطے لازم ہوتی ہے اور عملیات میں غالب گمان کافی ہے تو حق عمل میں ان شرابوں کا قیمتی ہونا ساقط ہے۔
Explanation: The sixth difference is that the sale of khamr is void, and the sale of other intoxicating drinks is valid. (7) There is no compensation for the destruction of khamr, whereas there is compensation for the destruction of other intoxicating drinks. However, this is the madhhab of Imam Sahib. According to the Sahibayn, their sale is also not valid, and there is no compensation for their destruction. But Imam Sahib says that other intoxicants are valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), and it has not been proven by a definitive proof that they are not valuable property, unlike khamr, whose value is nullified. Therefore, its sale is void, and whoever destroys it owes no compensation. But a Muslim cannot use that which is haram. So when compensation is due, it will not be in kind, but rather the price of the intoxicants will be compensated. However, both share the ruling that just as benefiting from khamr is not permissible, similarly all forms of benefiting from other intoxicants are haram, because all of them are also haram. It is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf that the sale of other intoxicants besides khamr is only valid when they have reduced by less than two-thirds and more than half. Warning: The author of Nata'ij al-Afkar has raised an objection here: Since all these are haram and no form of benefiting from them is permissible, then how can they be valuable? While scholars have clarified that the meaning of mal mutaqawwim is that benefiting from it is legally permissible. So when benefiting is not permissible, then value [taqawwum] is also non-existent. Moreover, a definitive proof is necessary for belief, but in practical matters, prevalent assumption is sufficient. So in practical reality, the value of these drinks is nullified.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal status of intoxicating drinks other than khamr (wine). It begins by stating the sixth difference: the sale of khamr is void, while the sale of other intoxicants is valid according to Abu Hanifa. Similarly, destroying khamr incurs no compensation, but destroying other intoxicants does. However, this is only Abu Hanifa's view. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the sale of these other intoxicants is also invalid, and no compensation is due for their destruction. Abu Hanifa argues that other intoxicants are mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) because no definitive proof (dalil qat'i) has established otherwise, unlike khamr whose value is explicitly nullified by divine texts. Therefore, selling them is valid, and compensation is due in terms of their market price, not in kind, because Muslims cannot engage in the substance itself. Despite this, both schools agree that all forms of benefiting (intifa') from any intoxicant are haram, since they are all impure and prohibited. A narration from Abu Yusuf states that the sale of non-khamr intoxicants is only permissible when they have reduced by between half and two-thirds through cooking. The commentator then addresses an objection from the author of Nata'ij al-Afkar: if benefiting is haram, how can they be considered mal mutaqawwim? The Hanafi response, implied in the passage, is that the definition of mal mutaqawwim for practical legal transactions (e.g., liability) is not strictly tied to permissibility of use; rather, the market value and recognition in civil dealings suffice. The authority weight of Abu Hanifa's ruling here is from the zahir al-riwayah, as it is the view preferred in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Specifically wine made from grapes, considered the most severe intoxicant with definitive prohibition.
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Property that has recognized value in Shari'ah, making transactions valid and destruction compensable.
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability to compensate for loss or destruction of another's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the sale of intoxicating drinks other than khamr?
A) Valid, because they are mal mutaqawwim
B) Invalid, because they are not mal mutaqawwim
C) Valid only if the drink has reduced by half
D) Valid only if the buyer is a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
The sale of non-khamr intoxicants is invalid according to the mufta bihi (Sahibayn), and no compensation is due for their destruction, though Abu Hanifa allowed it due to their perceived value.
