The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute on Non-Grape Intoxicants
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on non-grape intoxicants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on non-grape intoxicants, identify the mufta bihi, and apply the ruling to the consequences of hadd and talaq.

Core Idea
Non-grape intoxicants: halal (Abu Hanifa) vs haram (Muhammad) – the fatwa follows Muhammad.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding intoxicating drinks made from wheat, barley, honey, and corn. Imam Abu Hanifa considered them halal, while Imam Muhammad declared them haram. We will parse the text from Al-Jami' al-Saghir, understand the legal consequences for hadd and talaq, and learn why the later fatwa adopted Muhammad's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling in the text
Locate the statement 'there is no harm in other drinks' from Al-Jami' al-Saghir. Note that this is Abu Hanifa's position.
2
Contrast with Muhammad's contrary narration
Find the second riwaya from Muhammad that declares such drinks haram and imposes hadd and talaq.
3
Understand the legal effects
Under Abu Hanifa: no hadd even if drunk, and talaq is invalid (like a sleeping person). Under Muhammad: hadd applies and talaq is valid.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The text explicitly states the fatwa is on Imam Muhammad's view. Confirm this with later Hanafi sources like Shaami.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الجامع الصغير وما سوى ذلك من الاشربة فلا بأس به قالوا هذا الجواب على هذا العموم والبيان لا يوجد في غيره وهو نص على ان ما يتخذ من الحنطة والشعير والعسل والذرة حلال عندابی حنيفة ولا يحد شاربه عنده وان سكر منه ولا يقع طلاق السكران منه بمنزلة النائم ومن ذهب عقله بالبنج ولين الرماک
ترجمہ اور محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا ہے اور جو ان کے علاوہ شراب ہیں اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ یہ جواب اس عموم اور بیان کے مطابق جامع صغیر کے غیر میں موجود نہیں ہے اور یہ جواب نص ہے اس بات پر کہ جو شراب بنائی جاتی ہے گیہوں سے اور جو سے اور شہد اور جوار سے حلال ہے ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اور ان کے نزدیک اس کے پینے والے پر حد نہیں ماری جائے گی اگر چہ اس سے مست ہو جائے اور اس کے سکر ان کی طلاق واقع ہوگی نائم کے درجہ میں اور جیسے وہ شخص کہ جس کی عقل زائل ہوگئی ہو بھنگ کی وجہ سے اور گھوڑی کے دودھ کی وجہ سے۔
تشریح امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں یہ عبارت ارشاد فرمائی ہے جو گذری جس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اقسام اربعہ مذکورہ کے علاوہ جو اور شراب ہیں جیسے گیہوں کی اور ہو وغیرہ کی اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اس پر فخر الاسلام وغیرہ نے شروح جامع صغیر میں فرمایا ہے کہ جیسے امام

محمد نے عمومی انداز یہاں اختیار فرمایا ہے اپنی دوسری کتابوں میں یہ عموم بیان نہیں کیا ہے پھر امام صاحب کے نزدیک اقسام اربعہ مذکور کے علاوہ جو شراب ہیں وہ حلال ہیں اور ان کے پینے والے پر حد نہ ہوگی اگر چہ نشہ بھی ہو جائے اور ان کو پینے کی وجہ سے اگر مست ہو کر طلاق دیگا تو طلاق بھی واقع نہ ہوگی جیسے سونے والا طلاق دے یا وہ شخص طلاق دے جو بھنگ یا گھوڑی کے دودھ سے متأثر ہو گیا اور اس کی عقل زائل ہو گئی۔
وعن محمد انه حرام ويحد شاربه اذا سكر منه ويقع طلاقه اذا سكر منه كما في سائر الاشربة المحرمة
And he said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'And besides that, from the drinks there is no harm.' They said: 'This answer with this generality and exposition is not found in other [books]. And it is a text that what is made from wheat, barley, honey, and corn is halal according to Abu Hanifa, and the one who drinks it is not punished with hadd even if he becomes intoxicated from it, and the divorce of an intoxicated person from it does not occur; he is like a sleeping person, and like one whose mind has gone insane due to bhang and mare's milk. Translation: And Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'And besides those drinks there is no harm.' The mashayikh said: 'This answer with this generality and exposition is not found in other [works]. And this answer is a text that what is made from wheat, barley, honey, and corn is halal according to Abu Hanifa, and in his view the drinker is not punished with hadd even if he becomes intoxicated, and the divorce of one intoxicated from it does not occur; he is in the category of a sleeping person, and like one whose mind has been removed due to bhang and mare's milk. Explanation: Imam Muhammad stated this expression in al-Jami' al-Saghir, whose meaning is that besides the mentioned four types, other drinks like those from wheat, etc., there is no harm. Upon this, Fakhr al-Islam and others in commentaries on al-Jami' al-Saghir said that Imam Muhammad adopted a general style here, which he did not express in his other books. Then in Imam Sahib's view, besides the four types, other drinks are halal, and their drinker is not subject to hadd even if intoxicated, and if he divorces while intoxicated from it, the divorce does not occur, like a sleeping person divorcing, or a person whose mind has been affected by bhang or mare's milk. And from Muhammad [in another narration] that it is haram, and its drinker is punished with hadd if he becomes intoxicated from it, and his divorce occurs if he becomes intoxicated from it, as in all prohibited drinks.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir of Imam Muhammad regarding drinks other than the four types (khamr from grapes, nabidh from dates, etc.). The core statement is: 'There is no harm in other drinks.' The mashayikh note that this general permission is unique to this book. According to Abu Hanifa, drinks from wheat, barley, honey, and corn are halal (lawful) to consume. Consequently, the drinker is not subject to the hadd punishment for intoxication, even if he becomes drunk. Moreover, if he divorces his wife while intoxicated from such drinks, the divorce does not take effect; he is treated as if asleep or insane (e.g., from bhang or mare's milk). However, Imam Muhammad also has another narration (riwaya) stating that all such drinks are haram, and the drinker is subject to hadd and his divorce valid, like any prohibited intoxicant. The author notes that the fatwa is on Imam Muhammad's second view (haram). The passage thus shows a clear intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa (halal) and Muhammad (haram) on non-grape intoxicants.

Key Terms
حد (ḥadd): Fixed punishment for drinking intoxicants, specifically 80 lashes for free men.
سكران (sukrān): One who is intoxicated from a substance. Here, if from a halal drink, talaq does not occur; if from haram, talaq occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in this lesson, what is the ruling on intoxicating drinks made from wheat and honey?
A) Halal, no hadd
B) Haram, hadd applies if intoxicated
C) Makruh only
D) Halal but hadd applies

Key Takeaway
Non-grape intoxicants are haram in the operative Hanafi position (Muhammad's view), with hadd and valid talaq upon intoxication.

Abu Yusuf's Return to Abu Hanifa's View on Non-Grape Intoxicants
Intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf's initial conditional view and his return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition Abu Yusuf initially set for prohibition of non-grape drinks and explain why he later returned to Abu Hanifa's view.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf's conditional prohibition based on ten-day stability was later abandoned in favour of Abu Hanifa's general permissibility.

In this lesson, we examine a fascinating intra-Hanafi development: Imam Abu Yusuf's initial conditional ruling on non-grape intoxicants and his subsequent return to the position of Abu Hanifa. This passage from Al-Jami' al-Saghir illustrates how even the great disciples refined their positions, and it highlights the Hanafi methodology of evaluating intoxicants based on their essential property rather than contingent signs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi views
Note that Abu Hanifa holds non-grape intoxicants are halal, Imam Muhammad holds they are haram unconditionally, and Abu Yusuf initially had a conditional prohibition (haram if it remains unspoiled for ten days after fermentation). Then he returned to Abu Hanifa's view.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf that later resolved.
2
Explain the condition
The condition is that the drink 'remains after it ferments for ten days and does not spoil' (yamkuthu ba'da ma yablughu 'ashrata ayyam wa la yafsud). The meaning of 'yablugh' is that it becomes strong (yashidd), and 'la yafsud' is that it does not become sour (la yahmud).
The condition indicates strength and potential intoxication.
3
Analyse the reasoning behind the condition
The passage explains that the drink's stability for ten days without turning sour is evidence of its strength and intensity, which is a sign of its intoxicating property. Thus, it serves as a marker for prohibition.
This is an indirect way of inferring intoxication from a physical sign.
4
Trace Abu Yusuf's return
Abu Yusuf later abandoned his conditional view and reverted to Abu Hanifa's position that such drinks are halal. The commentary notes that his first view was like Imam Muhammad's (haram) but with the added condition.
This shows that even a mujtahid can change his view when the stronger evidence becomes apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال فيه ايضاً وكان ابو يوسف يقول ما كان من الاشربة يبقى بعد ما يبلغ عشرة ايام ولا يفسد فاني اكرهه ثم رجع الى قول ابى حنيفة وقوله الاول مثل قول محمد ان كل مسكر حرام الا انه تفرد بهذا الشرط ومعنى قوله يبلغ يغلى ويشتد ومعنى قوله ولا يفسد لا يحمض ووجهه ان بقاء هذه المدة من غيران يحمض دلالة قوته وشدته فكان أية حرمته ومثل ذلك مروى عن ابن عباس رضى الله عنهما
ترجمہ اور محمد نے جامع صغیر میں یہ بھی کہا ہے کہ ابو یوسف فرمایا کرتے تھے کہ جواشر بہ میں سے جوش مارنے کے بعد دس دن تک باقی رہے اور خراب نہ ہو تو میں اس کو مکر وہ جانتا ہوں ( حرام جانتا ہوں ) پھر ابو حنیفہ کے قول کی جانب رجوع کر لیا اور ابو یوسف کا پہلا قول محمد کا قول ہے کہ ہر مسکر جرائم مگر ابو یوسف اس شرط میں متفرد ہیں اور محمد کے قول يبلغ کے معنی یغلی اور میشند کے ہیں اور ان کے قول ولا يفسد کے معنی لا یمحض کے ہیں اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس مدت تک بقاء بغیر کھٹا ہوئے اس کی قوت وشدّت کی دلیل ہے تو یہ اس کی حرمت کی نشانی ہے اور اسی کے مثل ابن عباس سے مروی ہے۔
And he said in it also: Abu Yusuf used to say: 'Of the drinks, that which remains after it ferments for ten days and does not spoil, I consider it makruh (or haram).' Then he returned to Abu Hanifa's view. And his first view is like the view of Muhammad: that every intoxicant is haram. However, he was unique in this condition. The meaning of his word 'yablugh' (ferments) is that it boils and becomes strong. And the meaning of his word 'la yafsud' (does not spoil) is that it does not become sour. And its justification is that its remaining for this period without becoming sour is evidence of its strength and intensity, so it is a sign of its prohibition. And something similar is narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage narrates the evolution of Abu Yusuf's position on non-grape intoxicants. Initially, he held that any drink that remains unspoiled for ten days after active fermentation is makruh (i.e., haram). This condition was his own addition. His first view essentially agreed with Imam Muhammad's general rule that every intoxicant is haram, but with the added condition of ten-day stability. However, he later retracted (raja'a) to Abu Hanifa's view that such drinks are halal. The commentator explains the terminology: 'yablugh' means it bubbles and becomes strong (ferments), and 'la yafsud' means it does not become sour (vinegar). The rationale is that the drink's ability to remain strong without turning sour indicates its potency, which is a sign of its intoxicating property and hence prohibition. This condition is also reported from Ibn Abbas. The lesson thus shows Abu Yusuf's shift from a stricter conditional prohibition to acceptance of Abu Hanifa's broader permissibility.

Key Terms
يبلغ (yablugh): The drink ferments, boils, and becomes strong.
لا يفسد (lā yafsud): The drink does not spoil or turn into vinegar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition did Abu Yusuf set for a non-grape drink to be considered haram?
A) It intoxicates immediately
B) It remains unspoiled for ten days after fermentation
C) It is made from dates
D) It is cooked until half evaporates

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's initial conditional prohibition of non-grape intoxicants was abandoned in favour of Abu Hanifa's general permissibility, illustrating the process of ijtihad and reconciliation within the Hanafi school.

Abu Hanifa's Ruling on Non-Four-Type Intoxicants
States Imam Abu Hanifa's position on non-four-type intoxicants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on non-four-type intoxicants, identify the consequences for hadd and divorce, and distinguish the position of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, only four types of intoxicants are prohibited; others are halal with no legal penalties.

This lesson examines Imam Abu Hanifa's position on intoxicating beverages beyond the four types of khamr. While later Hanafi authority follows Imam Muhammad's stricter view, understanding Abu Hanifa's original ruling clarifies the evolution of the school's position and highlights the internal ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of khamr
Recall the four specific types of wine (khamr) that are unanimously prohibited: grape wine, date wine, raisin wine, and honey wine.
These are the 'aqsam arba'ah' referenced in the text.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa rules that all intoxicants other than these four are halal. Consequently, the drinker is not subject to hadd, and divorce pronounced while intoxicated by such beverages is invalid.
This is analogous to a sleeping person or one affected by non-intoxicating substances.
3
Understand the rationale
The ruling is based on the principle that only specific intoxicants are explicitly prohibited in the primary texts. Other intoxicants remain permissible by default.
This contrasts with a more general prohibition based on the concept of intoxication.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that all intoxicants are haram and subject to hadd, and divorce is valid when intoxicated. Abu Yusuf initially agreed but later retracted to Abu Hanifa's view.
This is a legitimate difference within the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Imam Muhammad. Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., in Shami) give precedence to his view due to caution.
Thus, in practice, the stricter view is applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الاشربة
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴
محمد نے عمومی انداز یہاں اختیار فرمایا ہے اپنی دوسری کتابوں میں یہ عموم بیان نہیں کیا ہے پھر امام صاحب کے نزدیک اقسام اربعہ مذکور کے علاوہ جو شراب ہیں وہ حلال ہیں اور ان کے پینے والے پر حد نہ ہوگی اگر چہ نشہ بھی ہو جائے اور ان کو پینے کی وجہ سے اگر مست ہو کر طلاق دیگا تو طلاق بھی واقع نہ ہوگی جیسے سونے والا طلاق دے یا وہ شخص طلاق دے جو بھنگ یا گھوڑی کے دودھ سے متأثر ہو گیا اور اس کی عقل زائل ہو گئی۔
Book of Beverages. Ashraf al-Hidaya: Urdu commentary of Hidaya, volume 14. Muhammad has adopted a general approach here; he did not state this generality in his other books. Then, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, besides the mentioned four types, whatever wines there are, they are halal, and there will be no hadd (fixed punishment) on the one who drinks them, even if intoxication occurs. And if due to drinking them he becomes drunk and gives divorce, the divorce will not take effect, like a sleeping person giving divorce or a person who, affected by bhang or mare's milk, loses his intellect.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary. It begins by noting that Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) adopted a general approach here in his Jami' al-Saghir, but did not state this generality in his other books. Then it clarifies the position of Imam Abu Hanifa: besides the four specific types of intoxicants (khamr, etc.), all other intoxicating beverages are halal. Consequently, the drinker is not subject to the hadd penalty (fixed punishment for intoxicated drinking), and if he becomes intoxicated and pronounces divorce, the divorce is not valid, similar to a divorce from a sleeping person or someone whose intellect is impaired by non-intoxicating substances like bhang or mare's milk.

Key Terms
اشربة (ashribah): Beverages, specifically alcoholic drinks in this context.
حد (hadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Sharia for specific crimes, here for intoxication.
اقسام اربعہ (aqsam arba'ah): The four specific types of wine (khamr) that are haram by consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are haram and subject to hadd
B) They are halal, and no hadd applies even if intoxication occurs
C) They are makruh
D) They are halal only if not intoxicating

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's original ruling on non-four-type intoxicants differs from the later adopted school position; the mufta bihi follows Imam Muhammad's stricter view.

Imam Muhammad's Position on All Intoxicants
States Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's dissenting view that all intoxicants are haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad's position on non-four-type intoxicants, identify the mufta bihi, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute's practical consequences.

Core Idea
Fatwa relies on Imam Muhammad: All intoxicants are haram, subject to hadd and valid divorce.

This passage presents the dissenting view of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani within the Hanafi school. While Imam Abu Hanifa considered only specific types of intoxicants as prohibited, Imam Muhammad extends the prohibition to all intoxicants. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for knowing the operative ruling in Hanafi fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Imam Abu Hanifa's view (non-four-type intoxicants are halal) and Imam Muhammad's view (all intoxicants are haram).
2
Note the legal consequences
Under Muhammad's view, drinking to intoxication triggers hadd (80 lashes) and valid divorce.
Intoxication (sukr) is a condition for both hadd and divorce effect.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The text explicitly states fatwa is upon Muhammad's view, citing Shami (Radd al-Muhtar).
This is the operative position for fatwa today.
4
Compare with other schools
This aligns with the Shafi'i position, making it not only an intra-Hanafi matter.
Shafi'is also consider all intoxicants haram.

وعن محمد انه حرام ويحد شاربه اذا سكر منه ويقع طلاقه اذا سكر منه كما في سائر الاشربة المحرمة ترجمہ اور محمد سے منقول ہے کہ یہ سب حرام ہیں اور اس کے پینے والے کو حد ماری جائے گی جبکہ اس سے مست ہو جائے اور اس کی طلاق واقع ہوگی جبکہ اس سے مست ہو جائے جیسا کہ ان تمام شرابوں پر جو حرام ہیں۔ تشریح جو ماقبل میں گذرا وہ امام صاحب کا قول تھا اور امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ بھی حرام ہیں اور جو ان کو پئے گا تو اس پر حد جاری ہو گی بشرطیکہ نشہ ہو اور اسی طرح جب شرابوں کو پی کر مست ہو اور بیوی کو طلاق دی تو اس کی طلاق بھی واقع ہوگی اور تمام حرام شرابوں کا یہی حکم ہے۔

تنبیہ فتوی امام محمد کے قول پر ہے ملاحظہ ہو شامی ص ۲۹۲ ج ۵
Imam Muhammad's perspective on the mentioned issue. And it is narrated from Muhammad that it is haram, and its drinker is flogged (hadd) when he becomes intoxicated from it, and his divorce takes effect when he becomes intoxicated from it, just like all other prohibited beverages. Translation: And it is narrated from Muhammad that all these are haram, and its drinker will be given hadd when he becomes intoxicated from it, and his divorce will take effect when he becomes intoxicated from it, like all these beverages that are haram. Commentary: What passed before was the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, and Imam Muhammad says that these are also haram, and whoever drinks them will have hadd applied provided intoxication occurs, and likewise when he becomes intoxicated after drinking these drinks and divorces his wife, his divorce also takes effect. And the same ruling applies to all prohibited beverages. Note: The fatwa is upon Imam Muhammad's statement; see Shami, vol. 5, p. 292.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This lesson presents Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's dissenting position within the Hanafi school. The Arabic text quotes directly from Muhammad: these beverages (the non-four-type intoxicants) are haram, the drinker receives the hadd penalty if intoxicated, and if he divorces while intoxicated from them, the divorce is valid. The commentary explains that this is in contrast to the previously stated position of Imam Abu Hanifa, and then emphasizes that the relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Imam Muhammad, citing the authoritative Hanafi reference, Radd al-Muhtar (Shami).

Key Terms
حرام (haram): Prohibited; carries legal consequences of sin, punishment, and effect on acts like divorce.
حد (hadd): Fixed penalty for intoxication; here 80 lashes according to Hanafi school.

Abu Yusuf's Conditional View on Intoxicants and His Retraction
Describes Abu Yusuf's initial condition-based view and his return to Abu Hanifa's position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on non-grape intoxicants and state why the mufta bihi remains Muhammad's unconditional prohibition despite Abu Yusuf's retraction.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf's 10-day condition was retracted; the operative view is Muhammad's: all intoxicants are haram.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute about non-grape intoxicants. Imam Abu Yusuf initially proposed a unique condition—a beverage is haram only if it remains unspoiled for ten days after fermentation, based on an athar from Ibn Abbas and a rational sign of strength. But he later retracted this and returned to Abu Hanifa's more permissive view. Understanding this retraction clarifies the development of Hanafi fiqh and the authority of the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi positions
List the views of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and the initial/retracted view of Abu Yusuf regarding non-grape intoxicants.
Focus on the conditions each imam attached to prohibition.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's condition
Abu Yusuf required that the drink remain for ten days after fermentation without becoming sour. This stability was seen as evidence of strength and thus prohibition.
The key terms 'yablugh' (ferment strongly) and 'la yafsud' (not sour) define the condition.
3
Analyze the rationale and evidence
The condition was justified by an athar from Ibn Abbas and by a logical argument: stability indicates potency, which signals intoxication and therefore prohibition.
The commentary notes that this athar is not found in major hadith collections.
4
Note the retraction and its implication
Abu Yusuf himself retracted his condition-based view and adopted Abu Hanifa's position, acknowledging its weakness.
This illustrates that ijtihad can be revised when stronger evidence appears.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Despite the retraction, the relied-upon fatwa position remains Muhammad's unconditional prohibition of all intoxicants, not Abu Hanifa's permissive view.
The earlier lesson established that the mufta bihi is Muhammad's stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام ابو یوسف کا اپنے پہلے قول سے رجوع

وقال فيه ايضاً وكان ابو يوسف يقول ما كان من الاشربة يبقى بعد ما يبلغ عشرة ايام ولا يفسد فاني اكرهه ثم رجع الى قول ابى حنيفة وقوله الاول مثل قول محمد ان كل مسكر حرام الا انه تفرد بهذا الشرط ومعنى قوله يبلغ يغلى ويشتد ومعنى قوله ولا يفسد لا يحمض ووجهه ان بقاء هذه المدة من غيران يحمض دلالة قوته وشدته فكان أية حرمته ومثل ذلك مروى عن ابن عباس رضى الله عنهما

ترجمہ اور محمد نے جامع صغیر میں یہ بھی کہا ہے کہ ابو یوسف فرمایا کرتے تھے کہ جواشر بہ میں سے جوش مارنے کے بعد دس دن تک باقی رہے اور خراب نہ ہو تو میں اس کو مکر وہ جانتا ہوں ( حرام جانتا ہوں ) پھر ابو حنیفہ کے قول کی جانب رجوع کر لیا اور ابو یوسف کا پہلا قول محمد کا قول ہے کہ ہر مسکر جرائم مگر ابو یوسف اس شرط میں متفرد ہیں اور محمد کے قول يبلغ کے معنی یغلی اور میشند کے ہیں اور ان کے قول ولا يفسد کے معنی لا یمحض کے ہیں اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس مدت تک بقاء بغیر کھٹا ہوئے اس کی قوت وشدّت کی دلیل ہے تو یہ اس کی حرمت کی نشانی ہے اور اسی کے مثل ابن عباس سے مروی ہے۔ تشریح یعنی جامع صغیر ہی میں امام محمد نے یوں بھی فرمایا ہے کہ پہلے امام ابو یوسف کا مسلک یہ تھا کہ یہ شرابیں جوش داشتداد کے بعد جب دس روز تک رہ جائیں اور خراب نہ ہوں تو اس کو حرام جانتے تھے پھر امام ابو یوسف نے امام صاحب کے قول کی طرف رجوع کر لیا اور امام ابو یوسف کا پہلا قول امام محمد کے قول کے مثل ہے کہ ہر سکر حرام ہے ۔ یعنی امام محمد کے نزدیک ہر مسکر حرام ہے اور یہی پہلے ابو یوسف فرمایا کرتے تھے یعنی جو قول ان کا ابھی ذکر ہوا ہے اس کاحال یہی ہے

مگر ابن عباس کا یہ اثر کہیں نہیں ملا۔
Abu Yusuf's retraction from his first statement. And he said therein also: Abu Yusuf used to say: 'Whatever beverages remain after they have fermented for ten days and do not spoil, then I consider them disliked (i.e., haram).' Then he retracted to the statement of Abu Hanifa. And his first statement is like Muhammad's statement: that every intoxicant is haram. However, he was unique in this condition. The meaning of his word 'yablugh' is 'it ferments and becomes strong', and the meaning of his word 'la yafsud' is 'it does not become sour'. The reason is that remaining for this period without becoming sour indicates its strength and intensity, so it is a sign of its prohibition. And similar to that is narrated from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both). Translation: And Muhammad also said in al-Jami' al-Saghir that Abu Yusuf used to say: 'Among beverages, when after fermentation it remains for ten days and does not spoil, I consider it makruh (meaning haram).' Then he retracted to the statement of Abu Hanifa. And Abu Yusuf's first statement is like Muhammad's statement: 'Every intoxicant is haram.' But Abu Yusuf is unique in this condition. The meaning of Muhammad's word 'yablugh' is 'it ferments and becomes strong', and his word 'la yafsud' means 'it does not become sour'. The reason is that surviving this period without becoming sour is evidence of its strength and intensity, so it is a sign of its prohibition. And similar to that is narrated from Ibn Abbas. Commentary: That is, in al-Jami' al-Saghir, Imam Muhammad also said that initially the school of Abu Yusuf was that these beverages, after fermentation, when they remain for ten days and do not spoil, he considered them haram. Then Abu Yusuf retracted to the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa. And Abu Yusuf's first statement is like Muhammad's statement that every intoxicant is haram. That is, according to Muhammad, every intoxicant is haram, and this is what Abu Yusuf used to say initially. So the statement of his that is now mentioned is the same. But this narration of Ibn Abbas has not been found anywhere.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This lesson details the position of Imam Abu Yusuf and his retraction. Initially, Abu Yusuf held that beverages that ferment and remain unspoiled for ten days are haram, similar to Imam Muhammad's general prohibition of all intoxicants. He added a condition: the drink must not sour within ten days, as stability indicates strength and thus prohibition, a view attributed to Ibn Abbas. However, Abu Yusuf later retracted this condition-based view and reverted to Imam Abu Hanifa's position, which allows non-four-type intoxicants as halal. The commentary notes that this specific narration (athar) from Ibn Abbas is not found in the main Hadith collections. The lesson explains the rationale behind the condition and highlights the fluidity of early Hanafi ijtihad.

Key Terms
يبلغ (yablugh): In this context, it means the beverage ferments and becomes strong (yaghli wa yashtadd).
لا يفسد (la yafsud): Does not spoil or turn sour (la yahmid).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's condition-based view was retracted; the mufta bihi is Muhammad's unconditional prohibition of all intoxicants.

Abu Hanifa's Criterion of Real Intensity
Defines the threshold for prohibition using real intensity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of haqiqat al-shiddah and apply it to determine when a non-khamr beverage becomes haram in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A beverage is haram only when it shows real intensity through vigorous frothing, not by mere age or trace alcohol.

Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) established a precise physical criterion for when a non-khamr beverage becomes prohibited. Unlike his students who used time-based conditions, Abu Hanifa insisted on observing a tangible sign of intoxication: vigorous frothing. This lesson unpacks that criterion and its legal implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the beverage and its legal category
Determine whether the drink is khamr (grape wine) or non-khamr (like date wine). Abu Hanifa's criterion applies to both, but non-khamr is the main focus.
2
Understand the criterion: haqiqat al-shiddah
Real intensity means the drink has fermented to the point of vigorous frothing, indicating strong intoxicating potential. This is a physical sign, not a time duration.
3
Apply the ruling
If the drink has not shown real intensity, it remains permissible (mubah). Once it froths vigorously, its original drinking becomes haram, and intoxication from it is also haram.
4
Contrast with other views
Abu Yusuf initially used a 10-day condition, while Muhammad had a broader view. But Abu Hanifa's reliance on observable intensity is the standard in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام ابوحنیفہ خمر اور غیر خمر میں حقیقی شدت کا اعتبار کرتے ہیں

وابو حنيفة يعتبر حقيقة الشدة على الحد الذي ذكرناه فيما يحرم اصل شربه وفيما يحرم السكر منه على ما تذكره ان شاء الله تعالى

ترجمہ اور ابوحنیفہ اعتبار کرتے ہیں حقیقی شدت اس حد کی مقدار جر میم نے ذکر کردی ہے اس خراب ہی کی اس کا اصل شرب حرام ہے اور اس میں جس کا سکر حرام ہے اس تفصیل کے مطابق کہ جس کو ہم انشاء اللہ بیان کریں گے۔ تشریح امام صاحب تحر اور غیر خمر میں حقیقی شدت کا اعتبار کرتے ہیں جیسا کہ گذر گیا کہ جوش داشتند اد کے ساتھ جھاگ بھی مارے تب حرام ہوگی خمر ہو یا غیر خمر دونوں میں یہی حد مقرر ہے اس کی مزید تفصیل اور آئے گی ۔
Abu Hanifa considers real intensity to the limit we mentioned regarding what is prohibited in its original drinking and what is prohibited due to intoxication, as we will mention, God willing. Translation: And Abu Hanifa considers real intensity to the limit that we have mentioned in that which its original drinking is haram, and in that which its intoxication is haram, according to the detail that we will explain, God willing. Commentary: Imam Abu Hanifa considers real intensity in khamr and non-khamr. As has passed: when it ferments and bubbles vigorously, then it becomes haram, whether khamr or non-khamr; this is the established limit. More detail will follow.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the criterion used by Imam Abu Hanifa to determine when a beverage becomes prohibited. The key is 'real intensity' (haqiqat al-shiddah), which is observed when the drink ferments and vigorously froths (josh mar ke jhag da). This applies equally to khamr (grape wine) and non-khamr (other intoxicants). Unlike the time-based condition of Abu Yusuf, Abu Hanifa relies on a tangible sign of intoxication. The commentary indicates that this is the established limit in the school, and further details will follow later.

Key Terms
حقيقة الشدة (haqiqat al-shiddah): Real intensity; the physical state of vigorous fermentation and frothing that indicates intoxicating strength.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What physical sign does Abu Hanifa use to determine prohibition of non-khamr beverages?
A) Age of the drink
B) Real intensity (vigorous frothing)
C) Smell of alcohol
D) Color change

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's criterion of real intensity ensures that a beverage is only prohibited when it actually becomes intoxicating, not based on arbitrary time periods.

Abu Yusuf's Final Retraction
Confirms Abu Yusuf's retraction from his condition-based view fully to Abu Hanifa's position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the significance of Abu Yusuf's retraction and identify the remaining intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on intoxicants.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf retracted to Abu Hanifa's view that not all intoxicants are haram, yet fatwa still follows Muhammad.

This passage records a critical moment in Hanafi juristic history: Imam Abu Yusuf's final retraction. After initially requiring a condition for prohibition, he returned fully to Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion. However, this retraction did not resolve the school's ikhtilaf, as Imam Muhammad's contrary view remains the basis for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions in this dispute
Abu Hanifa: non-four-type intoxicants are not haram. Abu Yusuf: initially required a spoilage condition, then retracted to Abu Hanifa. Muhammad: all intoxicants are haram.
The retraction (ruju') means Abu Yusuf joined the Sheikhayn position.
2
Analyse the significance of Abu Yusuf's ruju'
Ruju' is accepted in Hanafi methodology when a mujtahid revises his ijtihad. Here, Abu Yusuf abandoned both his earlier ruling and his condition, fully adopting Abu Hanifa's view.
This does not automatically make the school unanimous; Muhammad's view still stands.
3
Determine the mufta bihi position
Despite the Sheikhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) agreeing, the operative ruling for fatwa is Imam Muhammad's stricter view that all intoxicants are haram.
Tarjih authority: the later Hanafi masters gave weight to Muhammad's position as a precaution.

Source Text

امام ابو یوسف کار جوع

وابو يوسف رجع الى قول ابي حنيفة فـلـم يـحـرم كل مسكر و رجع عن هذا الشرط ايضا ترجمہ اور ابو یوسف نے ابو حنیفہ کے قول کی طرف رجوع کر لیا پھر انہوں نے ہر مسکر کو حرام قرار نہیں دیا اور اس شرط سے بھی رجوع کرلیا۔ تشریح یعنی امام ابو یوسف نے اپنے قول مذکور سے اور ساتھ ساتھ شرط مذکور سے کہ دس دن تک رکھنے سے خراب نہ ہو، اس سے رجوع فرما کر اس کے قائل ہوئے جو امام صاحب کا مسلک ہے کہ مذکورہ اشر یہ حرام نہیں ہیں ۔
Abu Yusuf's retraction. And Abu Yusuf retracted to the statement of Abu Hanifa, so he did not consider every intoxicant haram, and he also retracted from this condition. Translation: And Abu Yusuf retracted to the statement of Abu Hanifa, then he did not declare every intoxicant as haram, and he also retracted from this condition. Commentary: That is, Imam Abu Yusuf retracted from his mentioned statement and also from the mentioned condition (that it does not spoil after ten days), and became a proponent of what Imam Abu Hanifa's school is: that the mentioned beverages are not haram.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This brief lesson confirms the final position of Imam Abu Yusuf. He completely moved away from his earlier view (which required a 10-day spoilage condition for prohibition) and adopted Imam Abu Hanifa's stance: not every intoxicant is haram. This means that beverages other than the four types remain halal according to Abu Yusuf's final position, aligning fully with Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction; Abu Yusuf's return to Abu Hanifa's opinion.
مسکر (muskir): Intoxicant; specifically non-four-type intoxicating beverages in this lesson.
شیخین (Shaykhayn): The two primary imams: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (after his retraction).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They are haram unconditionally
C) They are not haram
D) He remained silent

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's retraction united him with Abu Hanifa, but the school's fatwa still follows Imam Muhammad's stricter view on intoxicants.

Lightly Cooked Nabidh: Sheikhayn vs Muhammad
States the ruling on lightly cooked date and raisin nabidh with Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the Sheikhayn permit lightly cooked nabidh and distinguish their position from that of Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Dates and raisins nabidh: lightly cooked, non-intoxicating amount, no frivolity — halal per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This lesson examines an internal Hanafi dispute regarding lightly cooked nabidh (infusion) from dates or raisins. While Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i consider it unconditionally haram, the Sheikhayn—Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf—permit it under specific conditions. The ruling is analogized to the case of muthallath (triple-cooked grape syrup).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sheikhayn's ruling
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: lightly cooked date or raisin nabidh is halal.
This is the position recorded in al-Mukhtasar by al-Quduri.
2
List the conditions for permissibility
Identify the three conditions: (1) light cooking, (2) drinking an amount that one is confident will not intoxicate, (3) no frivolity or amusement.
The text says 'yaghlibu ʿalā ẓannihi annahu lā yuskiru' — dominant opinion it won't intoxicate.
3
Identify the opposing view
Imam Muhammad (and al-Shafi'i) hold it haram unconditionally, even if lightly cooked and non-intoxicating.
Their dalil is the general prohibition of intoxicants, e.g., 'every intoxicant is khamr and every khamr is haram'.
4
Trace the analogy to muthallath
The Sheikhayn's reasoning is analogous to the case of triple-cooked grape syrup (muthallath), which is permissible because cooking removes the intoxicating potential.
This qiyas is based on the illa of loss of intoxicating property through cooking.

Source Text

نبیذ تمر اور نبیذ زبیب کو ہلکا سا پکانے اور پینے کا حکم

وقال في المختصر ونبيذ التمر والزبيب اذا طبخ كل واحد منهما ادنى طبخة حلال وان اشتد اذا شرب منه ما يغلب على ظنه انه لا يسكر من غير لهو ولا طرب ولهذا عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف وعند محمد والشافعي حرام والكلام فيه كالكلام في المثلث العنبي وتذكره ان شاء الله تعالى. ترجمہ اور قدوری نے مختصر میں فرمایا ہے اور نبیذ تمر اور نبیذ زبیب جبکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک کو ہلکا سا پکا دیا جائے تو حلال ہے اگر چہ جوش مارے جبکہ اس کو اپنے اتنی مقدار کہ اس کا ظن غالب یہ ہو کہ یہ نشہ نہیں کرے گا ( جبکہ پنے ) بغیر لہو سرور کے اور یہ شیخین کے نزدیک ہے اور محمد اور شافعی کے نزدیک حرام ہے اور کلام اس میں مثلث علمی کے اندر کلام کے مثل ہے اور ہم اس کو انشاءاللہ ذکر کریں گے۔ تشریح نبیذ تمرار نبیذ ز بیب جب ان کو ہلکا سا کا دیا جائے شیخین کے نزدیک حلال ہے لیکن پہلی شرط ہلکا سا پکانا ہے دوسری شرط اتنی
Ruling on lightly cooking and drinking nabidh of dates and raisins. And he said in al-Mukhtasar: 'Nabidh of dates and raisins, when each one of them is cooked with the lightest cooking, it is halal, even if it ferments, provided he drinks from it an amount such that his dominant opinion is that it will not intoxicate, without play or amusement. And this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is haram. And the discussion about it is like the discussion about the triple grape mixture (muthallath al-‘inabi), and we will mention it, God willing.' Translation: And Quduri said in al-Mukhtasar: 'Nabidh of dates and raisins, when each of them is lightly cooked, is halal, even if it ferments, provided he drinks from it an amount such that his dominant opinion is that it will not intoxicate, without play and amusement. And this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is haram. And the discussion about it is like the discussion about the triple grape mixture, and we will mention it, God willing.' Commentary: Nabidh of dates, nabidh of raisins – when they are lightly cooked, according to the Sheikhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is halal, but the first condition is light cooking, the second condition is [that amount]...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This lesson addresses the ruling on nabidh (infusion) made from dates or raisins that has been lightly cooked. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (the Sheikhayn), it is halal even if it ferments, as long as the drinker is confident it will not intoxicate and consumes it without frivolity. In contrast, Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i declare it haram. The commentary explains that the ruling is analogized to the case of 'muthallath al-‘inabi' (a triple-cooked grape syrup). The conditions for halal according to the Sheikhayn are: (1) light cooking, and (2) drinking only an amount that one is sure will not cause intoxication. The text is cut off, but the remaining discussion would elaborate.

Key Terms
نبیذ (nabidh): An infusion of fruits (dates or raisins) in water, which may ferment but can be made non-intoxicating through light cooking.
مثلث (muthallath): A triple-cooked grape syrup that is non-intoxicating and considered halal by the Sheikhayn.
الشيخين (al-Shaykhayn): The two senior imams: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (sometimes including Muhammad, but here distinguished).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Sheikhayn permit lightly cooked date/raisin nabidh if non-intoxicating, while Imam Muhammad forbids it unconditionally—a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf based on the illa of intoxication.

Abu Hanifa's Criterion for Real Intensity and Abu Yusuf's Retraction
Explains Abu Hanifa's criterion and Abu Yusuf's return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's criterion for real intensity (haqiqat al-shiddah) and explain why Abu Yusuf's initial condition was retracted.

Core Idea
A drink is haram only if it reaches real intensity—fermentation with frothing—not merely by remaining unchanged for ten days.

In this passage, we examine an important intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about the legal definition of intoxication. Abu Hanifa insisted on a visible sign—frothing—as the mark of real intensity, while Abu Yusuf initially considered a ten-day stability as evidence. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi school's precise approach to intoxicating beverages and the eventual convergence of the two imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Abu Hanifa's criterion (real intensity via frothing) and Abu Yusuf's initial condition (ten days without spoilage).
Both are within the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the evidence for each view
Abu Hanifa: frothing indicates real intensity. Abu Yusuf: ten-day stability indicates lasting strength.
The passage also mentions a weak narration from Ibn Abbas.
3
Analyze the retraction (ruju')
Abu Yusuf retracted his condition and returned to Abu Hanifa's view. The author notes that the narration from Ibn Abbas was not found.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Yusuf's earlier view, a drink unchanged for ten days would be haram; under the final Hanafi view, it remains halal unless it froths.
This affects the rulings for many traditional beverages.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's: only drinks with real intensity (fermentation and frothing) are haram.
Abu Yusuf's initial condition is marjuh.

Source Text

امام ابو یوسف کار جوع

That it is similar to Muhammad's statement. So Abu Yusuf had an extra condition not in Muhammad's statement: namely that the wine does not spoil and that a condition of ten days is placed because when the wine remains for ten days and does not become sour, that is evidence of its strength and intensity which is a sign of prohibition. This condition narrated from Abu Yusuf is similarly narrated from Ibn Abbas, but this athar of Ibn Abbas was not found elsewhere. Imam Abu Hanifa considers real intensity in khamr and non-khamr. Abu Hanifa considers real intensity to the extent we mentioned: regarding that whose original drinking is haram and regarding that whose intoxicant is haram according to the detail we will mention, God willing. Commentary: Imam Sahib considers real intensity in khamr and non-khamr, as passed: when it ferments and also froths, then it becomes haram whether khamr or non-khamr, the same limit is fixed. More detail will come. Abu Yusuf's return: Abu Yusuf returned to Abu Hanifa's statement, so he did not declare every intoxicant haram and also returned from this condition. Commentary: That is, Imam Abu Yusuf retracted his previous statement and also the mentioned condition (that it does not spoil by being kept for ten days) and adopted the position of Imam Sahib that these drinks are not haram.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the criterion for a drink becoming haram due to intoxication. It begins by referencing a statement similar to Imam Muhammad's, then explains that Abu Yusuf added a condition of a ten-day delay without spoilage as evidence of strength. The author notes a weak narration from Ibn Abbas. The core issue is Abu Hanifa's definition of 'real intensity' (haqiqat al-shiddah): the drink must ferment and froth; mere strength without frothing is not enough. Abu Yusuf initially required a ten-day period but later retracted, agreeing with Abu Hanifa that not every intoxicant is haram—only those with this specific intensity. The passage follows the author's order: first Abu Hanifa's principle, then Abu Yusuf's condition, then his return. All rulings are from the Hanafi school, with the relied-upon position being Abu Hanifa's.

Key Terms
حقیقت الشدة (haqiqat al-shiddah): The real intensity of a drink indicated by fermentation and frothing, as opposed to mere strength or longevity.
غیر موجود (ghayr mawjud): Not found; the author says the narration from Ibn Abbas was not found elsewhere, indicating weakness.

Key Takeaway
Only drinks that reach real intensity (fermentation with frothing) are haram according to the Hanafi school; Abu Yusuf's extra condition of ten days was retracted.

Ruling on Lightly Cooked Date and Raisin Nabidh
States the ruling on lightly cooked nabidh of dates and raisins with a Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for the permissibility of lightly cooked date/raisin nabidh according to the Shaykhayn and explain why Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i differ.

Core Idea
Lightly cooked nabidh is halal if it does not intoxicate and is drunk for strength, not amusement.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a key intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute over nabidh from dates and raisins. While the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) permit it under specific conditions, Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i forbid it entirely. Understanding the conditions is essential for applying the Hanafi ruling in practice and distinguishing it from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The ruling concerns date or raisin nabidh that is lightly cooked (adna tabkhah). According to the Shaykhayn, it is halal under conditions; according to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is haram.
Note that the act is drinking lightly cooked nabidh, not raw nabidh.
2
List the three conditions for halal according to the Shaykhayn
1) The nabidh must be lightly cooked (adna tabkhah; slightest cooking). 2) One drinks only an amount that one is strongly certain does not intoxicate. 3) One drinks without amusement or merriment (lahw wa tarab), but rather for bodily strength.
These conditions restrict the permission to avoid harm and frivolity.
3
Note the dispute: Shaykhayn vs. Muhammad and al-Shafi'i
Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i consider even lightly cooked nabidh that froths (ishtadda) as haram unconditionally. They view frothing itself as a sign of intoxication potential.
The Hanafi school relies on the Shaykhayn's position as the mufta bihi.
4
Understand the analogy to grape methuselah
The author mentions that the ruling on lightly cooked nabidh parallels the case of grape juice cooked until two-thirds evaporates (mithlath 'inabi). The same dispute applies there and will be discussed later.
This analogy shows consistency in the school's reasoning across similar beverages.

Source Text

نبیذ تمر اور نبیذ زبیب کو ہلکا سا پکانے اور پینے کا حکم
وقال في المختصر ونبيذ التمر والزبيب اذا طبخ كل واحد منهما ادنى طبخة حلال وان اشتد اذا شرب منه ما يغلب على ظنه انه لا يسكر من غير لهو ولا طرب ولهذا عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف وعند محمد والشافعي حرام والكلام فيه كالكلام في المثلث العنبي وتذكره ان شاء الله تعالى. ترجمہ اور قدوری نے مختصر میں فرمایا ہے اور نبیذ تمر اور نبیذ زبیب جبکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک کو ہلکا سا پکا دیا جائے تو حلال ہے اگر چہ جوش مارے جبکہ اس کو اپنے اتنی مقدار کہ اس کا ظن غالب یہ ہو کہ یہ نشہ نہیں کرے گا ( جبکہ پنے ) بغیر لہو سرور کے اور یہ شیخین کے نزدیک ہے اور

The ruling on lightly cooking and drinking date and raisin nabidh. He said in al-Mukhtasar: Nabidh of dates and raisins, when each of them is cooked the slightest cooking, is halal, even if it froths, when one drinks from it an amount that one is strongly sure does not intoxicate, without amusement and merriment. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is haram. The discussion about it is like the discussion about the grape methuselah and we will mention it, God willing. Translation: And Quduri said in al-Mukhtasar: Nabidh of dates and raisins, when each of them is cooked the slightest cooking, is halal, even if it froths, when one drinks from it an amount that one is strongly sure does not intoxicate, without amusement and merriment. And this is according to the two shaykhs. According to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is haram. The discussion about it is like the discussion about the grape methuselah and we will mention it, God willing. Commentary: Nabidh of dates and raisins, when they are lightly cooked, is halal according to the two shaykhs, but first condition: lightly cooking; second condition: drinking an amount that does not intoxicate; third condition: the purpose of drinking is not amusement and merriment but rather bodily strength. According to Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i, it is haram, just as when grape water is cooked and its two-thirds evaporate, there is the same difference which will be mentioned later.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding lightly cooked nabidh of dates and raisins. The author cites al-Mukhtasar by al-Quduri, stating the ruling: such nabidh is halal for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn) if three conditions are met: (1) it is lightly cooked (adna tabkhah), (2) one drinks only an amount that one is sure does not intoxicate, and (3) one drinks without amusement or merriment (lahw wa tarab), but rather for strength. Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i consider it haram unconditionally. The author likens this dispute to the similar one regarding 'mithlath 'inabi' (grape matter cooked until two-thirds evaporates), which will be discussed later. The commentary expands on the conditions and the difference of opinion, noting that the Hanafi position (Shaykhayn) permits with the three conditions, while the opposing view (Muhammad and Shafi'i) forbids it. The passage follows the author's order: first the ruling from al-Mukhtasar, then the translation, then commentary clarifying the conditions.

Key Terms
نبیذ (nabidh): An infusion of dates, raisins, or other fruits, often left to ferment; in this context, lightly cooked versions are discussed.
ادنی طبخہ (adna tabkhah): The slightest cooking; light heating so that the drink changes but does not become fully cooked.
اشتد (ishtadda): It became strong or frothy; a sign of fermentation that may indicate intoxication potential.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling on lightly cooked date nabidh that froths but is drunk in a small amount without amusement?
A) Haram unconditionally
B) Halal with specific conditions
C) Makruh
D) Permissible only if cooked thoroughly

Key Takeaway
Lightly cooked date or raisin nabidh is halal in the Hanafi school (Shaykhayn) if lightly cooked, drunk in non-intoxicating amounts, and for strength rather than amusement.

Ruling on Khalitayn (Mixed Dates and Grape Drink)
Ruling on khalitayn with supporting hadith and resolution of apparent contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khalitayn and identify the evidence from the hadith of Ibn Umar, and explain how the apparent conflicting hadith is reconciled.

Core Idea
Mixed date and grape drink (khalitayn) is halal when cooked; the prohibition was a drought measure, not absolute.

This passage addresses a practical question: is it permissible to drink a beverage made by mixing dates and grapes (or raisins)? You might have heard a hadith prohibiting such mixing. The Hanafi school, based on the practice of Ibn Umar, clarifies that the prohibition was temporary and context-specific. The key is that the drink must be cooked, which removes the intoxicating potential that was the original concern.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling on khalitayn
State clearly whether the school considers it halal or haram based on the passage.
The passage begins with 'there is no harm' indicating permissibility.
2
Examine the primary evidence
Read the narration from Ibn Ziyad about Ibn Umar giving him a drink. Understand what this story demonstrates.
Ibn Umar himself confirms it was khalitayn and that it was cooked.
3
Analyze the condition: cooking
Note that the permissibility is linked to the drink being cooked. The passage compares it to the uncooked infusion (naqi' al-zabib) which is prohibited.
The condition is that it must be lightly cooked; otherwise it becomes like the prohibited uncooked drink.
4
Reconcile the conflicting hadith
The passage cites a hadith prohibiting mixing dates and raisins. Explain how the author resolves this by interpreting it as specific to a time of drought.
This is a classic Hanafi method: haml 'ala halat al-shiddah (interpretation according to a situation of hardship).
5
Formulate the operative ruling
Combine the evidence and reconciliation to state the final fiqh ruling: khalitayn is halal if lightly cooked, and the prohibition was abrogated or contextual.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is permissibility.

خلیطین کی حقیقت اور انکا حکم

سے یہ واقعہ بیان کیا تو فرمانے لگے ہم نے تجھے کھجور اور کشمش سے زیادہ کچھ نہیں پلایا اس کو لیسطین کہتے ہیں اور یہ پکی ہوئی بھی تھی ۔ دلیل جس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ابن عمر سے مروی ہے کہ کشمش و غیرہ کا کچا پانی حرام ہے تو ان کی اس بات سے واضح ہو گیا کہ یہ مطبوخ تھی ورنہ وہ ابن زیاد کو کیسے پلاتے۔ سوال آپ فلسطین کو جائز بتارہے ہیں حالانکہ حدیث میں اس کی ممانعت آئی ہے کہ انگور اور چھوارے اور انگور اورکھجور اور گدر کھجورکو

دوسرے غریبوں کو دیدو۔
The reality and ruling of khalitayn. He said: There is no harm in khalitayn because of what is narrated from Ibn Ziyad that he said: Ibn Umar gave me a drink, and I almost did not find my way to my family. The next day I went to him and informed him of that. He said: We only gave you 'ajwa (dates) and raisins, and this is from khalitayn, and it was cooked. Because what is narrated from him is the prohibition of the infusion of raisins, which is the uncooked water of it. And what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade combining dates and raisins, raisins and fresh dates, and fresh dates and unripe dates is interpreted as the time of severity and that was in the beginning. Translation: Quduri said: There is no harm in khalitayn (mixed dates and grapes) because of what is narrated from Ibn Ziyad: Ibn Umar gave me a drink such that I almost did not reach my family. The next day I came to him and told him, so he said: We only gave you dates and raisins, and this is khalitayn, and it was cooked. Because what is narrated from Ibn Umar is the prohibition of the infusion of raisins, which is the uncooked water of it. And it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade combining dates and raisins, raisins and fresh dates, and fresh dates and unripe dates; this is interpreted as the time of drought and that was in the beginning of Islam. Commentary: The reality of khalitayn is that dates and grapes or raisins and munakki are mixed to make a drink, and it is halal provided it is lightly cooked, as Ibn Ziyad said that Ibn Umar gave him a drink that made him unable to go home. The next day he told him, so Ibn Umar said: We only gave you dates and raisins, this is khalitayn, and it was cooked. The evidence is that it is narrated from Ibn Umar that the uncooked infusion of raisins is haram, so this shows that it was cooked, otherwise he would not have given it to Ibn Ziyad. Question: You say khalitayn is permissible, but there is a hadith prohibiting mixing grapes and dried dates? Answer: This prohibition was in early Islam during a drought, aiming to prevent hoarding so that the poor could have enough.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding khalitayn, a drink made by mixing dates and grapes/raisins. The author begins by stating that there is no harm in it, citing a narration from Ibn Ziyad: he was given such a drink by Ibn Umar that made him intoxicated, and Ibn Umar confirmed it was khalitayn and was cooked. The key point is that the drink was cooked, avoiding the prohibition of uncooked raisin infusion (naqi' al-zabib). The author then addresses an apparent contradiction: a hadith prohibits combining dates and raisins. The resolution is that this prohibition was in early Islam during a time of drought (shiddah), to discourage hoarding and encourage charity, not because it is intrinsically haram. The commentary adds that khalitayn is halal provided it is lightly cooked, and the hadith is interpreted contextually. The passage follows the author's order: the ruling, the proof (hadith), the resolution of the conflicting hadith, and the commentary explaining the conditions.

Key Terms
خلیطین (khalitayn): A drink made by mixing dates and grapes or raisins together.
حمل على حالة الشدة (haml 'ala halat al-shiddah): Interpretation of a prohibition as applying to a specific situation of hardship (drought) rather than as a general rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Permissibility of Uncooked Nabidh from Non-Grape Sources
States rule on uncooked nabidh of honey, figs, wheat, corn, barley for Hanafis with hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on uncooked nabidh from honey, figs, and grains, and explain the hadith proof that distinguishes these from khamr.

Core Idea
Uncooked nabidh from honey, figs, and grains is halal because khamr is specifically from grapes and dates.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a question that often arises in Hanafi fiqh: what about drinks made from sources other than grapes and dates? Al-Quduri states that such nabidh is halal even uncooked, according to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), provided it is not drunk for amusement or merriment. The proof is a hadith that explicitly defines khamr (the prohibited intoxicant) as coming only from the grapevine and date palm, so drinks from other sources do not fall under the same prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the passage and identify the act being ruled upon (uncooked nabidh of honey, figs, wheat, corn, barley) and its explicit ruling (halal/mubah).
The ruling is stated at the beginning: 'Nabidh of honey and figs, and nabidh of wheat, corn, and barley is halal even if not cooked.'
2
Note the Condition Attached
The permissibility is conditional: 'when it is without amusement and merriment (lahw wa tarab).' Understand that this implies the drink should not be consumed in a context of frivolity or for intoxication.
The condition distinguishes lawful use from unlawful consumption.
3
Locate the Dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'Khamr is from these two trees' — referring to the grapevine and date palm. This is the proof for the ruling.
The hadith is a sahih or hasan narration, classified as zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalala in the Hanafi view.
4
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (Legal Inference)
The hadith specifies that khamr (the intoxicant that is haram) is exclusively from grapes and dates. Since honey, figs, grains are not from these sources, their infusions cannot be khamr, and thus are not prohibited as khamr.
Hanafi usul holds that a specific textual exclusion ('khass') overrides general prohibition.
5
Consider Cross-Madhhab Implications
The Shafi'i school generally considers any intoxicating drink as khamr, while the Hanafi school limits the definition to grape and date wine. This affects the permissibility of non-grape alcoholic drinks in small quantities.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is significant in fatwa.

شہد ، انجیر ، گیہوں، جوار، جو کی نبیذ کا حکم
قال ونبيذ العسل والتين ونبيذ الحنطة والذرة والشعير حلال وان لم يطبخ وهذا عندابي حنيفة وابي يوسف رحمهما الله اذا كان من غير لهو وطرب لقوله عليه السلام الخمر من هاتين الشجرتين واشار الى الكرمة نخلة خص التحريـ ــــــــــا والــمــراد بيان الحكم ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور شہداور انجیر کی نبیذ اور گیہوں اور جوار اور جوکی نیز حلال ہے اگر چہ پکائی نہ جائے اور یہ شیخین کے نزدیک
والـ
The ruling on nabidh of honey, figs, wheat, corn, barley. He said: Nabidh of honey and figs, and nabidh of wheat, corn, and barley is halal even if not cooked, and this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may God have mercy on them, when it is without amusement and merriment, because of his saying, peace be upon him: "Khamr is from these two trees" and he pointed to the grapevine and date palm. ... and the intended meaning is to clarify the ruling. Translation: Quduri said: And nabidh of honey and figs, and nabidh of wheat, corn, and barley is also halal even if not cooked, and this is according to the two shaykhs. And...

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding uncooked nabidh made from honey, figs, wheat, corn, and barley. The author cites al-Quduri's statement that such nabidh is halal even if not cooked, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn), provided that it is not drunk for amusement or merriment (lahw wa tarab). The proof is a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Khamr is from these two trees' — referring to the grapevine and date palm — indicating that khamr (the intoxicant that is haram) is specifically from grapes and dates, not from other sources like honey or grains. Therefore, nabidh from other sources does not become khamr and remains halal as long as it is not intoxicating or drunk for amusement. The commentary would expand that the Shaykhayn's position is that such drinks are halal uncooked, but the text is cut off. The passage follows the author's order: the ruling from al-Mukhtasar, the proof from hadith, and a brief translation.

Key Terms
نبیذ العسل (nabidh al-asal): An infusion of honey in water.
خص الخمر من هاتين الشجرتين (khassa al-khamr min hataayn al-shajaratayn): The hadith specifying that khamr (the prohibited intoxicant) is from the grapevine and date palm exclusively.

Ruling on Mixed Date and Raisin Drink (Khalitayn)
States permissibility of mixed date-raisin drink with evidence from Ibn Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khalitayn, identify the condition of cooking, and explain how the apparent contradictory hadith is interpreted within the school.

Core Idea
Khalitayn is permissible if cooked; the prohibition hadith applied to raw mixtures or a temporary drought context.

This lesson examines a specific beverage: dates and raisins mixed together. The Hanafi school, relying on a direct report from Ibn Umar, permits it under one key condition. We will walk through the evidence and see how an apparently conflicting hadith is reconciled through contextual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is being ruled upon? State the act and its legal classification (mubah) according to the text.
The act is drinking khalitayn, a mixture of dates and raisins (or grapes) that is lightly cooked.
2
Locate the Primary Evidence
Find the main dalil: the report from Ibn Ziyad about Ibn Umar. Quote the key statement from the source.
Ibn Umar gave a drink and then confirmed it was khalitayn and that it was cooked.
3
Analyze the Condition
What condition distinguishes permissible from impermissible? Why is cooking significant?
The condition is that the mixture is 'matbukh' (cooked). Raw infusion (naqi‘) of raisins is narrated as prohibited.
4
Reconcile the Conflicting Hadith
How does the text deal with the hadith that forbids mixing dates and raisins? Identify the interpretive move.
The prohibition hadith is interpreted as applying to a time of drought (shidda) and the early period of Islam, not a general permanent rule.
5
Determine Practical Application
Apply the ruling: What are the conditions for a modern drink to be considered permissible khalitayn?
Must be cooked (pasteurization suffices) and non-intoxicating. Raw mixtures remain impermissible.

قال ولا باس بالخليطين لماروى عن ابن زياد انه قال سقانى ابن عمر شربة ما كدت اهتدى الى اهلى فغدوت اليه من الغد فاخبرته بذلک فقال ما زدناک على عجوة وزبيب وهذا من الخليطين وكان مطبوخا لان المروى عنه حرمة نقيع الزبيب وهو النثى منه وماروى انه عليه السلام نهى عن الجمع بين التمر والزبيب والزبيب والرطب والبسر محمول على حالة الشدة وكان ذلك في الابتداء۔ ترجمہ: قدوری نے فرمایا اور خلیطین (کھجور اور انگور ملے ہوئے) میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے بوجہ اس کے کہ ابن زیاد سے مروی ہے کہ ابن عمر نے مجھے ایک شربت پلایا قریب نہیں تھا میں اپنے اہل تک پہنچ جاؤں پھر میں اگلے روز ابن عمر کے پاس گیا پس میں نے ان کو اس کی خبردی تو فرمایا نہیں زیادہ کیا ہم نے تجھ کو کھجور اور شمش پر اور یہ غلطین ہے اور یہ پکی ہوئی تھی اس لئے کہ ابن عمر سے نقع الزبیب کی حرمت مروی ہے اور یہ ستمش کا کچا پانی ہے اور حد مروی ہے نبی علیہ السلام سے کہ آپ نے منع فرمایا ہے جمع کرنے سے کھجور اور کشمش کے اور کشمش اور کھجور کے اور کھجور اور گدر کھجور کے درمیان یہ قحط کی حالت پر محمول ہے اور یہ بات ابتداء اسلام میں تھی۔ تشریح: خلیطین کی حقیقت یہ ہے کہ کھجور اور انگور یا کشمش و منقی کو ملا کر شربت بنایا جائے اور یہ حلال ہے بشرطیکہ ہلکا سا پکا دیا گیا ہو جیسا کہ ابن زیاد نے کہا ہے کہ مجھے ابن عمر نے ایک شربت پلایا اس سے میری یہ کیفیت ہوگئی کہ گھر تک جانا بھاری ہو گیا اگلے دن میں نے ان سے یہ واقعہ بیان کیا تو فرمانے لگے ہم نے تجھے کھجور اور کشمش سے زیادہ کچھ نہیں پلایا اس کو لیسطین کہتے ہیں اور یہ پکی ہوئی بھی تھی ۔ دلیل جس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ابن عمر سے مروی ہے کہ کشمش و غیرہ کا کچا پانی حرام ہے تو ان کی اس بات سے واضح ہو گیا کہ یہ مطبوخ تھی ورنہ وہ ابن زیاد کو کیسے پلاتے۔ سوال: آپ فلسطین کو جائز بتارہے ہیں حالانکہ حدیث میں اس کی ممانعت آئی ہے کہ انگور اور چھوارے اور انگور اورکھجور اور گدر کھجورکو ملا کر استعمال کیا جائے؟ جواب: یہ ممانعت ابتداء اسلام میں اس وقت تھی جبکہ قحط سالی کا زمانہ تھا اور مقصد یہ تھا کہ اکو جمع نہ کرو بلکہ ایک پر اکتفاء کرو اور دوسرے غریبوں کو دیدو۔
He said: There is no harm in khalitayn (the two mixed ones) because of what is narrated from Ibn Ziyad that he said: Ibn Umar gave me a drink such that I almost could not find my way to my family. So I went to him the next day and informed him of that. He said: I gave you nothing but dates and raisins, and this is from khalitayn, and it was cooked. Because what is narrated from him is the prohibition of the raw infusion of raisins, and that is the raw water of raisins. And what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade combining dates and raisins, and raisins and wet dates, and unripe dates and ripe dates, is interpreted as being during the time of hardship (drought) and that was in the early period (of Islam). Translation: Al-Quduri said: There is no harm in khalitayn (mixed dates and raisins) because of the narration from Ibn Ziyad that Ibn Umar gave me a drink such that I almost could not reach my family. Then the next day I went to Ibn Umar and informed him of that, so he said: I gave you nothing but dates and raisins, and this is khalitayn, and it was cooked. Because the prohibition of raw raisin infusion is narrated from Ibn Umar, and that is the raw water of raisins. And it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade combining dates and raisins, and raisins and fresh dates, and unripe dates and ripe dates; this is interpreted as being during the state of drought, and that was in the early period (of Islam). Commentary: The reality of khalitayn is that dates and grapes or raisins and sultanas are mixed together to make a drink, and it is permissible provided it is lightly cooked, as Ibn Ziyad said: Ibn Umar gave me a drink such that I had difficulty going home. The next day I told him this, and he said: I gave you nothing but dates and raisins; this is called khalitayn, and it was cooked. The proof they gave is that the raw water of raisins and other things is narrated to be prohibited from Ibn Umar, so it becomes clear that this was cooked, otherwise how would he give it to Ibn Ziyad. Question: You permit khalitayn while there is a hadith prohibiting mixing grapes and dried dates, grapes and dates, and unripe dates and ripe dates. Answer: This prohibition was in early Islam during a time of drought, and the purpose was that you should not combine them but rather be content with one and give the other to the poor.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the hadith on the permissibility of khalitayn (mixed drink of dates and raisins). The author (Al-Quduri, as commented upon) states that there is no harm in khalitayn, relying on the practice of Ibn Umar who gave such a drink to Ibn Ziyad. The key condition is that the mixture must be cooked (lightly boiled). The hadith that prohibits mixing dates and raisins is interpreted as applying to the early period of Islam during a drought, when the aim was to encourage frugality so that one type could be given to the poor. Thus, the Hanafi position is that khalitayn is halal. The proof is that Ibn Umar explicitly said it was 'khalitayn' and it was cooked, and he would not give something prohibited. This shows that the prohibition was specific to raw mixtures or to a temporary ruling.

Key Terms
خلیطین (khalitayn): A drink made by mixing dates and raisins (or grapes) together, often after light cooking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Haram
B) Makruh
C) Halal
D) Only permissible if raw

Key Takeaway
Khalitayn is permissible in the Hanafi school when cooked; the prohibition hadith is interpreted as specific to raw mixtures or a temporary context.

Nabidh from Honey, Figs, and Grains: Permissibility and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on nabidh from non-grape/non-date sources with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nabidh from non-grape/non-date sources, explain the proof from the hadith 'Khamr is from these two trees', and distinguish the two positions within the Hanafi school regarding the condition of cooking.

Core Idea
Khamr is only from grapes/dates; other nabidhs are halal unless consumed for pleasure (lahw wa tarab).

This passage clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: not every intoxicating drink is khamr. The Prophet ﷺ specified that khamr is exclusively from the grapevine and date palm. This ruling affects the permissibility of nabidh made from honey, figs, and various grains. The lesson also explores an internal Hanafi debate over whether cooking these drinks is a condition for their permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what drink is being ruled upon (nabidh from honey, figs, wheat, sorghum, barley) and the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: halal even if not cooked.
The ruling is conditional on the absence of lehw (diversion) and tarab (merriment).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the hadith cited: 'Khamr is from these two trees' – the Prophet ﷺ pointed to the grapevine and date palm. Understand that this specifies the prohibition of khamr to these two sources only.
The hadith is used to exclude other sources from the definition of khamr.
3
Analyse the condition of lehw wa tarab
Note the additional condition: the drink must not be consumed for diversion or merriment. If the intention is pleasure or intoxication, it becomes haram even if from a non-khamr source.
This condition aligns with the principle that means to haram are also haram.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on cooking
Identify the two positions: some Hanafis require cooking (boiling) for permissibility; others (including the author of Quduri) do not require it. The relied-upon view is that cooking is not a condition.
The reasoning for not requiring cooking: a small quantity of these drinks does not lead to a large intoxicating amount.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
Establish that the view without the cooking condition is mentioned in the books (e.g., Quduri) and is the preferred position for fatwa today.
This tarjih is based on the principle that the dalil (specific hadith) overrides general qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ونبيذ العسل والتين ونبيذ الحنطة والذرة والشعير حلال وان لم يطبخ وهذا عندابي حنيفة وابي يوسف رحمهما الله اذا كان من غير لهو وطرب لقوله عليه السلام الخمر من هاتين الشجرتين واشار الى الكرمة نخلة خص التحريم والــمــراد بيان الحكم۔ ترجمہ: قدوری نے فرمایا اور شہد اور انجیر کی نبیذ اور گیہوں اور جوار اور جو کی نیز حلال ہے اگر چہ پکائی نہ جائے اور یہ شیخین کے نزدیک ہے جبکہ بغیر لہو و سرور کے ہو نبی علیہ السلام کے فرمان کی وجہ سے خمر ان دونوں درختوں کی ہوتی ہے اور انگور اور کھجور کی جانب اشارہ کیا تو آپ نے تحریم کو ان دونوں کے ساتھ خاص فرمادیا اور مراد حکم کو بیان کرنا ہے۔ تشریح: یہ سب مذکورہ نبیذیں شیخین کے نزدیک حلال ہیں اگر چہ پکائی بھی نہ گئی ہوں۔ لیکن شرط یہ ہے کہ بغیر لہو و سرور کے قوت بدن کے لئے استعمال کرے، ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے کہ آپ صلى الله عليه وسلم بیان احکام کے لئے مبعوث ہوئے ہیں اور آپ نے جو ارشاد فرمایا ہے الخمر من هاتين ... الخ ۔ اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو شراب حرام نہیں وہ انگور اور کھجور سے بنائی جاتی ہیں۔ لہذا اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ جو شہد اور انجیر وغیرہ سے بنائی جائے تو وہ حرام نہ ہوگی۔ ثم قيل يشترط الطبخ فيه لا باحته وقيل لا يشترط وهو المذكور فى الكتب لان قليله لا يدعوا الى كثيرة كيف ما كان۔ ترجمہ: پھر کہا گیا ہے کہ اس میں پکانا شرط نہیں ہے اس کی اباحت کے لئے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ شرط ہے اور یہی کتاب میں مذکور ہے اس لئے کہ اس کا قلیل اس کے کثیر کی جانب داعی نہیں ہے جیسے بھی ہو وہ (یعنی مطبوخ اور غیر مطبوخ)۔ تشریح: شیخین کے نزدیک ان نبیذوں کی حلت کے لئے کیا پکانا بھی شرط ہے تو اس میں دو قول ہیں: 1. شرط ہے ورنہ پھر حلال نہ ہوگی۔ 2. شرط نہیں ہے جیسا کہ قدوری میں اس کی صراحت ہے۔ دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ ان کا قلیل کثیر کی جانب داعی نہیں ہے خواہ کچی ہوں خواہ پکادی گئی ہوں۔
He said: The nabidh of honey, and figs, and the nabidh of wheat, sorghum, and barley is permissible even if not cooked, and this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them) when it is without diversion and merriment, because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Wine (khamr) is from these two trees' and he pointed to the grapevine and the date palm. He specified the prohibition (to these two) and the intent is to clarify the ruling. Translation: Al-Quduri said: The nabidh of honey, figs, wheat, sorghum, and barley is permissible even if not cooked, and this is according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) when it is without diversion and merriment, due to the Prophet's (peace be upon him) statement: 'Khamr is from these two trees' and he pointed to the grapevine and the date palm, so he specified the prohibition to these two, and the intent is to explain the ruling. Commentary: All these mentioned nabidhs are permissible according to the two shaykhs even if not cooked. But the condition is that they are used for bodily strength without diversion and merriment. It has passed earlier that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was sent to explain rulings, and his statement 'khamr is from these two trees' means that the wine that is prohibited is only made from grapes and dates. Hence it is known that what is made from honey, figs, etc. is not prohibited. Then he said: It is said that cooking is not a condition for its permissibility, and it is said it is a condition, and this is what is mentioned in the books because its small quantity does not call to its large quantity, however it may be (i.e., cooked or uncooked). Translation: Then it is said that cooking is not a condition for its permissibility, and it is said that it is a condition, and this is mentioned in the books because its small quantity does not invite to its large quantity, however it may be (i.e., cooked or uncooked). Commentary: According to the two shaykhs, regarding the permissibility of these nabidhs, is cooking a condition? There are two sayings: 1. It is a condition, otherwise it would not be permissible. 2. It is not a condition, as is explicit in Quduri. The second point is that their small quantity does not invite to their large quantity, whether they are raw or cooked.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of nabidh made from honey, figs, wheat, sorghum, and barley. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), these are halal even if uncooked, provided they are not consumed for lehw and tarab (diversion and merriment) but for physical strength. Their proof is the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'Khamr is from these two trees' – the grapevine and date palm – indicating that khamr (which is prohibited) is exclusively from grapes and dates. Therefore, beverages made from other substances, such as honey and grains, are not khamr and are permissible. However, there is a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding whether cooking (boiling) is a condition for permissibility. The text notes two opinions: some say cooking is required, others say it is not. The latter view is mentioned in the books (including Quduri) and is supported by the reasoning that a small amount of these drinks does not lead to intoxication or invite to large amounts. This ruling applies only when the intention is not to seek intoxication or pleasure.

Key Terms
نبیذ (nabidh): An infusion or drink made by soaking fruits, grains, or honey in water, sometimes lightly cooked.
لهو وطرب (lahw wa tarab): Diversion and merriment; the intention to enjoy the drink for pleasure rather than for bodily strength.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what condition is required for nabidh from honey to be permissible?
A) It must be cooked
B) It must be drunk in a gathering
C) It must not be consumed for lehw and tarab
D) It must be made from dates only

Key Takeaway
Nabidh from honey, figs, and grains is halal in the Hanafi school when consumed for physical strength, not for pleasure, and cooking is not required.
