Hadd Punishment for Intoxication from Grain-Based Drinks
Ruling on whether hadd is applied for intoxication from grain-based nabidh, with preferred view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for intoxication from grain-based drinks and identify the evidence for the preferred view.

Core Idea
Hadd is applied for intoxication from any drink, including grains, based on a general narration from Imam Muhammad.

This passage addresses a debated intra-Hanafi question: does the fixed hadd punishment for intoxication apply to someone who becomes drunk on a drink made from grains like wheat or barley? While some early Hanafis exempted such drinks, the later preferred view affirms the application of hadd, relying on a narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and considerations of custom and public interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage asks whether hadd (80 lashes) is applied to a person who becomes intoxicated from a drink made from grains (e.g., barley beer).
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, not a cross-madhhab issue.
2
State the two conflicting views
Two positions are presented: (1) earlier view attributed to Faqih Abu Ja'far: no hadd, comparing it to hashish; (2) preferred view of the later mashayikh: hadd is applied.
3
Locate the evidence for the preferred view
The preferred view relies on a narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that anyone who becomes intoxicated from any drink is given hadd 'without specification' (min ghayr tafsīl).
This narration is an authoritative qawl of a major Hanafi imam.
4
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The mashayikh argued that in their time, sinners gather to drink these grain-based intoxicants just as they do for wine, even more so. Thus, the effective cause (illah) for hadd – intoxication leading to corruption – is present.
This is a application of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil) and consideration of 'urf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The preferred and operative position (mufta bihi) is that hadd is applied for intoxication from any substance, including grain-based drinks.
The source states 'al-ashah annahu yahidd' – the more correct view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل يحد في المتخذ من الحبوب اذا سكر منه قيل لا يحدو قد ذكرنا الوجه من قبل قالوا والاصح انه يحد فانه روى عن محمد فيمن سكر من الاشربة انه يحد من غير تفصيل وهذا لان الفساق يجتمعون عليه في ـاعهم على ائر الاشربة بل فوق ذلك۔ ترجمہ: اور کیا حد ماری جائے گی اس شراب میں جس کو دانوں سے بنایا گیا ہو جبکہ اس سے مست ہو جائے تو کہا گیا ہے کہ حد نہیں ماری جائے گی اور ہم اس کی وجہ ماقبل میں ذکر کر چکے ہیں مشائخ نے فرمایا اور اصح یہ ہے کہ حد ماری جائے گی اس لئے کہ محمد سے مروی ہے اس شخص کے بارے میں جو شرابوں سے مست ہو گیا کہ اس کو حد ماری جائے گی بغیر تفصیل کے اور یہ حد اس وجہ سے کہ فساق جمع ہوتے ہیں اس پر ہمارے زمانہ میں ان کے جمع ہونے کے مثل تمام شرابوں پر بلکہ اس سے بھی بڑھ کر۔ تشریح: گیہوں اور جو وغیرہ سے جو شراب بنائی جاتی ہے تو اگر اس کو پی کر نشہ ہو گیا تو حد ہوگی یا نہیں تو اس میں دو قول ہیں فقیہ ابو جعفر کہتے ہیں کہ حد نہ ہوگی کیونکہ یہ بھنگ اور گھوڑی کے دودھ کے درجہ میں ہے۔
And is hadd applied for that which is made from grains, when he becomes intoxicated from it? It was said: Hadd is not applied, and we have mentioned the reason before. They said: And the more correct is that hadd is applied, for it is narrated from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) concerning one who becomes intoxicated from drinks that he is given hadd without specification. And this is because sinners gather on it in their time similar to their gathering on all drinks, even more so. Translation: And will hadd be applied for that which is made from grains when one becomes intoxicated from it? It was said: Hadd will not be applied, and we have mentioned the reason previously. The mashayikh said: And the more correct is that hadd will be applied, because it is narrated from Muhammad concerning the person who becomes intoxicated from any drink that he is given hadd without distinction. And this is because sinners gather on it in our time similar to their gathering on all other drinks, even more so. Commentary: Regarding the drink made from wheat and barley, if one drinks it and becomes intoxicated, will hadd be applied? There are two sayings. Faqih Abu Ja'far says that hadd will not be applied because it is in the category of bhang (hashish) and mare's milk (which are not subject to hadd).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling with proof on whether the hadd punishment for intoxication (80 lashes) applies to someone who becomes drunk on a drink made from grains (like wheat or barley beer). The text presents two views: some earlier Hanafis (e.g., Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani) said no hadd, comparing it to hashish or mare's milk which are not traditional intoxicants subject to hadd. However, the preferred view (al-ashah) according to the later mashayikh is that hadd is applied, based on a narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani who gave a general ruling that anyone who becomes intoxicated from any drink receives hadd, without differentiating between sources. The reasoning is that in the later period (the time of the mashayikh), people gather to drink these grain-based intoxicants just as they gather for wine, and even more. Therefore, to prevent harm and corruption, the hadd punishment is applied. This reflects a move from a strict textual definition to a consideration of 'urf (custom) and maqsad (objective) of preventing intoxication.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): The fixed corporal punishment (80 lashes for intoxication) prescribed by shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a person who becomes intoxicated from a drink made from barley?
A) No hadd, because it is not khamr
B) Hadd is applied, according to the more correct view
C) Only if it is uncooked
D) Only if he drinks a large amount

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi preferred view applies hadd to intoxication from any drink, including grains, based on a general narration from Imam Muhammad and the consideration of custom and public interest.

Adding Water to Grape Juice Before Cooking Renders It Haram Even After Cooking
Ruling that adding water before cooking prevents halal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on grape juice mixed with water before cooking and explain the reasoning (talil) involving the property of water.

Core Idea
Adding water before cooking prevents halal transformation: the evaporated two-thirds must be from pure juice, not a mixture.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the purification of grape juice through cooking. The general rule is that cooking grape juice until two-thirds evaporates makes the remaining one-third halal (muthallath). However, if water is added before cooking, this process fails. The key insight is that water, being lighter, evaporates first, so the reduction does not guarantee that two-thirds of the juice itself has been removed. This ruling demonstrates the Hanafi principle of looking at the substance rather than the mixture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is adding water to grape juice before cooking. The ruling: the resulting drink is not halal (remains haram).
The act is not the cooking itself, but the sequence of adding water before cooking.
2
Locate the general principle
Recall the rule for pure grape juice (asir): if cooked until two-thirds evaporates, the remaining one-third (muthallath) becomes halal.
This is the baseline from which the exception is derived.
3
Identify the exception
The case where water is added before cooking is an exception: the drink does not become halal even if two-thirds of the total mixture evaporates.
The exception relies on the physical property of water.
4
Understand the talil (legal reasoning)
Water is lighter (latif) and evaporates first. Therefore, the two-thirds loss is not necessarily from the juice; it may be mostly water. Thus, the required reduction of pure juice has not occurred.
The reasoning is based on observable physical behavior, not arbitrary rule.
5
Distinguish the two-thirds measure
The measure for halal is two-thirds of the pure juice (thulutha al-asir), not two-thirds of the total mixture (thulutha al-kull). Only the former indicates proper purification.
This distinction is critical and often missed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انگور کے عرق میں پانی ڈالا پھر اس کو پکایا جس سے دو ثلث جل گیا آیا یہ حلال ہے یا نہیں ؟
بخلاف ما اذا صب الماء على العصير ثم يطبخ حتى يذهب ثلثا الكل لان الماء يذهب اولا للطافته اويذهب ـلايــكون الذاهب ثلث
ترجمہ بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ انگور کے عرق میں پانی ڈال دیا گیا پھر اس کو پکایا گیا یہاں تک کہ کل میں سے دو ثلث ختم ہو گئے اس لئے کہ پانی اپنی لطافت کی وجہ سے پہلے جلے گا یا ان دونوں میں سے جلے گا تو جلنے والا انگور کے پانی کا دونگٹ نہیں ہوا۔ تشریح اگر انگور کے عرق میں پہلے پانی ملا دیا اور پھر اسکو پکایا کہ دونٹ جل گیا اور ایک ثلث مجموعہ میں سے رہا تو یہ حلال نہ ہوگا کیونکہ مثلث حلال ہے اور یہاں یہ مثلث نہیں بنا کیونکہ مثلث تو وہ ہے کہ انگور کے عرق میں سے دونلت ختم ہو جائے اور یہاں پانی اور عرق کا دو ثمانی ختم ہوا ہے لہذا یہ مثالث نہیں ہے اور نہ حلال ہے۔
If one adds water to grape juice then cooks it until two-thirds of the whole goes away: contrary to this case, because water goes first due to its subtlety, or it goes together, so the one that goes is not two-thirds of the grape juice. Explanation: If water is mixed with grape juice first then cooked until two-thirds are gone and one-third remains, it will not be halal because the halal is the thulth (when two-thirds of the juice itself are gone), but here two-thirds of the whole mixture (water+juice) are gone, not two-thirds of the juice alone, so it is not thulth and not halal.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about grape juice ( عصير ) purification through cooking. The general rule is that if pure grape juice is cooked until two-thirds of it evaporates, the remaining one-third becomes halal (this is called 'thulth' or 'muthallath'). However, if water is added to the juice before cooking, this process does not work. Why? Because water is lighter and evaporates first, so the two-thirds that disappear may be mostly water, not the juice. Thus, the remaining liquid still contains more than one-third of the original juice, and it is not halal. The commentary explains that thulth specifically requires two-thirds of the pure juice to be gone. This ruling is based on the principle that the legal purification is attached to the specific substance (juice), not the mixture.

Key Terms
عصير (asir): Grape juice, the liquid squeezed from grapes.
مثلث (muthallath): Grape juice that has been cooked until two-thirds evaporates, leaving one-third; this is considered halal in Hanafi school.
ثلثا الكل (thulutha al-kull): Two-thirds of the entire mixture (water + juice) that evaporates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because two-thirds of the mixture is gone.
B) No, because water evaporates first, so the juice is not sufficiently reduced.
C) Yes, because water and juice are both pure liquids.
D) No, because mixing water with juice is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
Adding water before cooking prevents the formation of muthallath because water evaporates first; therefore, the drink remains haram even if two-thirds of the mixture boils off.

Two Narrations on Cooking Whole Grapes Before Squeezing
Two narrations from Abu Hanifa on cooking whole grapes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two narrations from Abu Hanifa on cooking whole grapes and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Cooking whole grapes does not change the rule: the juice inside requires two-thirds evaporation like after squeezing.

This passage addresses a subtle question: if grapes are cooked whole and then squeezed, does the same rule apply as when the juice is cooked? Imam Abu Hanifa has two transmitted positions. Understanding which one is relied upon is essential for correctly applying the ruling on intoxicating drinks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is cooking whole grapes (with juice inside) and then squeezing them to produce a drink. The question is whether this drink becomes halal after slight cooking or requires more extensive reduction.
2
State the two narrations
There are two riwayat from Abu Hanifa: one allows a slight cooking (adna tabkhah), the other requires two-thirds of the juice to evaporate (yadhhab thuluthahu).
3
Understand the reasoning
The first narration reasons that cooking whole grapes changes the substance, so a lighter rule applies. The second (al-ashah) argues that the juice inside is unchanged, so it is like cooking squeezed juice – the two-thirds rule applies.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The second narration is declared al-ashah (more correct) and is the operative position for fatwa. This is because it is more cautious and consistent with the general rule for grape juice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو طبخ العنب كما هو ثم يعصر يكتفى بادنى طبخة في رواية عن ابي حنيفة وفي رواية عنه لا يحل مالم يذهب ثلثاه بالطبخ وهو الاصح لان العصير قائم فيه من غير تغير فصار كما بعد العصر
ترجمہ اور اگر انگور جوں کا توں پکا دیا گیا پھر نچوڑا گیا تو ہلکے سے پکانے پر اکتفاء کر لیا جائے گا ابوحنیفہ سے ایک روایت کے مطابق اور ان سے دوسری روایت میں حلال نہ ہو گا جب تک کہ اس کا دونات ختم نہ ہو جائے پکانے کی وجہ سے اور یہی اصح ہے اس لئے کہ انگور میں شیرہ بغیر تغیر کے موجود ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے نچوڑنے کے بعد ۔ تشریح اگر بجائے شیرہ انگور کے انگور ہی کو پکا یا گیا اور بعد میں نچوڑ کر استعمال کیا جائے تو کیا حکم ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ اس میں امام صاحب سے دور روایتیں ہیں:۔ ہلکا سا کا نا کافی ہے -۲- اس کا حکم وہی ہے جو انگور کے عرق کا ہے یعنی اگر اتنا پکا دیا گیا کہ دونات عرق ختم ہو جائے تو حلال ہوگا ورنہ نہیں لہذا عرق انگور کا ایک ہی حکم ہے نچوڑنے سے پہلے بھی اور بعد بھی۔
And if grapes are cooked as they are then squeezed, a slight cooking is sufficient in one narration from Abu Hanifa, and in another narration from him it is not halal until two-thirds of it go away by cooking, and this is the more correct (al-ashah) because the juice (asir) remains in it without change, so it becomes like after squeezing. Explanation: If instead of the juice, the grapes themselves are cooked and then squeezed for use, what is the ruling? He said that there are two narrations from Imam Sahib: (1) A slight cooking is sufficient; (2) Its ruling is the same as that of grape juice, i.e., it becomes halal only if two-thirds of the juice is removed by cooking. So the ruling of grape juice is the same – both before and after squeezing.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the cooking of whole grapes (as opposed to juice). There are two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. One says that a slight cooking ( أدنى طبخة ) suffices because the grapes are cooked whole and then squeezed, so the cooking process is different. The second narration (considered the more correct, al-ashah) says that the same rule as for juice applies: the drink is halal only after two-thirds of the juice (now inside the grapes) has evaporated. The reasoning for the stronger view is that the juice remains present within the grapes unchanged, so it is as if the juice was squeezed first and then cooked. Therefore, the same conditions apply.

Key Terms
رواية (riwayah): A narration or transmission of a ruling from the Imam, possibly differing from another transmission.
الاصح (al-ashah): The more correct position among the transmitted views.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A slight cooking suffices.
B) Two-thirds of the juice must evaporate.
C) The grapes must be dried first.
D) It is always haram.

Key Takeaway
When whole grapes are cooked and then squeezed, the relied-upon Hanafi position requires two-thirds of the juice to evaporate for the drink to be halal.

Ruling on Mixing Grapes and Dates in Cooking: The Precautionary Application of the Stricter Rule
Ruling that when grapes and dates are cooked together, the stricter grape rule applies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mixture of grape juice and date infusion when cooked, and explain why the stricter grape rule is applied as a precaution.

Core Idea
When grape juice mixes with date infusion, the entire mixture falls under the stricter grape rule — precaution governs doubt.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario in the chapter of buyu (sales) concerning intoxicating beverages. While date infusion (naqi' al-tamr) becomes permissible with slight cooking, grape juice requires two-thirds evaporation. When these two are combined, the Hanafi school applies the principle of ihtiyat (precaution) and extends the grape juice rule to the entire mixture, requiring two-thirds evaporation for the mixture to become halal. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this cautious approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ingredients and their individual rules.
Recognize that date infusion (naqi' al-tamr) becomes halal with just a slight cooking, while grape juice requires two-thirds evaporation to become halal.
The base rulings are established elsewhere; here we apply them to a mixture.
2
Understand the difference in stringency.
The ruling for dates is lenient (light cooking suffices), while for grapes it is strict (two-thirds must evaporate). This difference triggers the precaution.
3
Apply the precautionary principle (ihtiyat).
When two substances with different rulings are mixed, the Hanafi school applies the stricter ruling to the entire mixture to ensure no impermissible element remains.
Ihtiyat is used in matters of doubt about halal/haram.
4
Note the textual caution (tanbih) about raisins.
The passage notes a possible error: the text mentions 'between dates and raisins', but raisins are not subject to the grape rule; this may be a scribal mistake.
The correct ruling for raisins is like dates, not grapes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جمع فى الطبخ بين العنب والتمراوبين التمر والزبيب لا يحل حتى يذهب ثلثاه لان التمران كان يكتفى فیه بادنى طبخة فعصير الغنب لا بدان يذهب ثلثاه فيعتبر جانب العنب احتياطا وكذا اذا جمع بين عصير
ترجمہ اور اگر پکانے میں انگور اور تمر یا تمر اور منفی کے درمیان جمع کر دیا گیا تو وہ حلال نہ ہوگا یہاں تک کہ اس کا دو ملت ختم ہو جائے اس لئے کہ تم اگر چہ اس میں ہلکے سے پکانے پر اکتفاء کر لیا جاتا ہے پس عصیر عنب اس کا دو ثلث ختم ہونا ضروری ہے تو بر بناء احتیاط انگور کی جانب کا اعتبار کیا جائے گا اور ایسے ہی جب جمع کر دیا جائے انگور کے رس اور نقیع تمر کے درمیان اسی دلیل کی وجہ سے جو کہ ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں ۔ تشریح اگر کھجور یا چھوارے پانی میں ڈالے گئے اور اس میں اشتد اردو سکر پیدا ہو گیا تو وہ بھی حرام ہے لیکن اگر اس کو پکا دیا جاتا تو یہ حرام نہ ہوتا اور اس کو ہلکا سا پکا نا کافی ہوتا دو ثلث کا ختم ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ حکم تو صرف انگور کے رس میں ہے لیکن اگر انگور کا رس اور ماتمر دونوں کو ایک جگہ ملا دیا تو اب کیسا حکم ہوگا تو فرمایا کہ بر بناء احتیاط مجموعہ پر انگور کا حکم جاری کیا جائے گا اور جب تک دونت نہ جل جائے جب تک وہ حلال نہ ہوگا۔
تنبیہ یہاں مصنف نے بین العنب والتمر کے بعد فرمایا ہے۔ اوبين التمر والزبيب. حالانکہ یہ صرف انگور اور خمر کاحکم ہے نہ کہ زبیب و تمر کا اس لئے کہ منفی انگور کے حکم میں نہیں ہے تو یہ مصنف یا نا خین و ناقلین کا سہو ہے كمالا يخفى
And if one combines in cooking between grapes and dates, or between dates and raisins, it is not halal until two-thirds of it goes, because concerning dates, a slight cooking was sufficient, but grape juice requires its two-thirds to go, so the grape side is considered as a precaution. Similarly, if one combines between grape juice and date infusion (naqi' al-tamr). Explanation: If dates or raisins are soaked in water and become intense and sweet, it is also haram, but if it is cooked, it becomes halal and a slight cooking suffices; the necessity of two-thirds loss is only for grape juice. But if grape juice and date infusion are mixed, then what? He said: as a precaution, the ruling of grapes is applied to the whole, and it will not be halal until two-thirds of it are gone. Note: Here the author mentioned 'between grapes and dates' and then 'between dates and raisins' although this is the ruling for grapes and khamr, not for raisins and dates, because raisins are not in the ruling of grapes. So this is an error of the author or the scribes and transmitters, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage deals with mixtures in cooking. For date infusion (naqi' al-tamr), a slight cooking makes it halal. For grape juice, two-thirds evaporation is needed. When these are mixed together, the precautionary principle (ihtiyat) dictates that the stricter rule (grapes) applies to the entire mixture. Thus, the whole must be reduced by two-thirds. The passage also contains a notable 'tanbih' (cautionary note) pointing out a probable textual error: the author mentioned between dates and raisins, but raisins are not in the same category as grapes (since raisins are dried grapes, but the rule for grape juice does not transfer to raisins; the rule for raisins is like dates). So this is flagged as a scribal mistake.

Key Terms
احتياطا (ihtiyatan): As a precaution; the stricter ruling is applied to remove any doubt.
نقيح (naqi'): An infusion, typically of dates or raisins soaked in water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It becomes halal as soon as it is cooked slightly.
B) It becomes halal only after two-thirds of the entire mixture evaporates.
C) It remains haram regardless of cooking.
D) The date portion becomes halal but the grape portion remains haram.

Key Takeaway
When grape juice and date infusion are mixed, the entire mixture requires two-thirds evaporation to become halal, due to the precautionary application of the stricter rule.

Adding Raw Fruit to a Cooked Infusion: The Ruling on Nabidh
Ruling on adding raw dates/raisins to a cooked infusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adding dates/raisins to a cooked infusion, identify the key condition (quantity), and apply the qiyas reasoning to similar cases.

Core Idea
When halal and haram combine, the haram dominates—even a cooked drink becomes haram if raw fruit is added in quantity.

In this passage, Marghinani addresses a subtle fiqh question: a date/raisin infusion was cooked slightly, making it halal. But what if more dates/raisins are added afterward? The answer depends on quantity. If negligible, it remains halal; if enough to produce nabidh, the whole becomes haram. The reasoning is based on a qiyas (analogy) to a known case, and the principle that when halal and haram mix, the haram aspect is given precedence (ghalabah al-hurmat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
The act is adding raw dates/raisins to a cooked infusion that was already halal. Determine the impact on the drink's legal status.
2
Pinpoint the decisive condition
The ruling hinges on whether the added amount is trivial or significant. Define 'significant' as an amount from which nabidh is normally produced.
3
Follow the qiyas (analogy)
The text analogizes this case to pouring a cup of raw naqi' into a cooked drink, which makes the whole haram. Identify the asl (original case), far' (new case), and illa (effective cause: the potential for intoxication).
The illa is the addition of a substance capable of causing intoxication in a quantity that can actually intoxicate.
4
Apply the principle of ghalabah
When halal and haram coexist, the haram is given precedence (ghalabah li al-hurmat). This principle justifies declaring the mixture haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو طبخ تقيع التمر والزبيب ادنى طبخة ثم انقع فيه تمرا وزبيب ان كان ما انقع فيه شيئا يسيرا لا يتخذ النبيذ من مثله لا باس به وان كان يتخذ النبيذ من مثله لم يحل كما اذا صب فى المطبوخ قدح من النقيع رمة
جهة الـ
ترجمہ اور اگر نقیع تمر اور نقیع زبیب کو ہلکا سا پکایا گیا پھر اس میں ثمر یا منفی ڈالی گئی اگر وہ مقدار جو اس میں ڈالی گئی ہے معمولی ہی ہو کہ اس کے مثل سے نبیذ بنائی جاتی ہو تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اور اگر اس کے مثل سے نبیذ بنائی جاتی ہے تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے جیسے مطبوخ میں جبکہ نقیع کا ایک پیالہ ڈالدیا گیا ہو اور سبب حرمت کی جہت کو غلبہ دینا ہے۔
تشریح نقیع تمر اور نقیع زبیب کو ہلکا پکا دیا گیا تھا جس کی وجہ سے وہ حلال ہوگئی تھی لیکن پکانے کے بعد اس میں پھر کھجور یا منتقی ڈالدی تو ب کیا حکم ہے؟ تو جواب دیا کہ اگر تمر یا منفی اتنی ڈالی ہے جس سے عادہ نبیذ نہیں بنائی جاتی یعنی بہت کم مقدار میں تو یہ تو عدم کے درجہ میں اور وہ بدستور حلال ہے۔ لیکن اگر اتنی ڈال دی جس سے عام طور سے نبیذ بنائی جاتی ہے تو اب وہ حلال نہیں بلکہ حرام ہو گئی ہے اور یہ بالکل ایسا ہے جیسے نبیذ تمر وغیرہ میں جبکہ وہ مطبوخ ہو جو کہ حلال ہے نقیع تمر کا ایک پیالہ ڈال دیا گیا تو اب ساری حرام ہو گئی ۔ اور وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ جہاں حلت و حرمت کا اجتماع ہوتا ہے وہاں غلبہ اور ترجیح حرمت کو ہوتی ہے۔
And if one cooks a date infusion and raisin infusion slightly, then soaks dates or raisins in it: if what is soaked in it is a small amount from which nabidh is not normally made, there is no harm; but if nabidh is made by its like, it is not halal, just as if one pours a cup of naqi' into the cooked drink. The direction is to give dominance to the cause of prohibition. Explanation: The infusion of dates and raisins was slightly cooked, which made it halal. But after cooking, if dates or raisins are added again, what is the ruling? He answered: if the added amount is so small that nabidh is not normally made from its like, then it is considered negligible and it remains halal. But if the amount is such that nabidh is normally made from it, then it becomes haram, similar to the case where a cup of raw date infusion is poured into a cooked drink that was halal, making it all haram. The reason is that when halal and haram coexist, the side of prohibition is given precedence.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a date/raisin infusion has been cooked slightly, making it halal (since for dates/raisins, a slight cooking suffices). Then, more dates or raisins are added after cooking. The ruling depends on the quantity added. If it is a mere trace, not enough to make nabidh by itself, then it is ignored, and the drink remains halal. But if it is an amount that would normally produce nabidh (intoxicating drink), then the entire mixture becomes haram because the added raw fruit introduces the potential for intoxication. This is analogized to pouring a cup of raw naqi' into a cooked halal drink: the whole becomes haram. The legal principle applied is: when halal and haram combine, the haram aspect is given precedence (al-ghalabah li al-hurmat).

Key Terms
نبيذ (nabidh): An intoxicating drink made from soaked dates or raisins (also other fruits).
غلبہ (ghalabah): Precedence given to the dominant attribute (here, the haram aspect).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It becomes makruh but not haram.
D) It becomes halal only if cooked again.

Key Takeaway
When halal and haram mix, the haram dominates; even a cooked infusion becomes haram if raw fruit is added in an amount that can produce intoxication.

Hadd for Nabidh and Cooking Wine
Rulings on hadd and cooking wine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why no hadd is applied for drinking precautionary nabidh and explain why cooking wine does not make it halal.

Core Idea
Hadd is averted by doubts; cooking does not undo the prohibition of khamr.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses two important rulings concerning intoxicating drinks. First, it clarifies that the earlier-mentioned nabidh, which was declared haram only as a precaution, does not incur the hadd punishment. Second, it reaffirms that once wine (khamr) becomes intensely intoxicating, even cooking until two-thirds evaporates does not make it permissible. Understanding these rulings requires distinguishing between prohibitions based on certainty and those based on precaution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and note the two separate legal statements: no hadd for nabidh, and cooking wine does not make it halal.
2
Analyze the reasoning for no hadd
The text says 'because the prohibition is precautionary'. Understand that hadd punishments require certainty and are averted by doubts (shubhat).
This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: hudud are averted by shubhat.
3
Analyze the reasoning for cooking wine
The text states that the prohibition has become 'established' (taqarrarat) and is not removed by cooking. This means the ruling is based on the text of the Quran and Sunnah, not on the physical property of the drink.
The term 'thuluthah' is used elsewhere for grape juice, but here it applies to wine that was already intoxicating.
4
Note the cross-madhhab significance
Some other schools might allow cooking wine to remove its intoxicating property, but the Hanafi school maintains the original prohibition remains.
This is a point of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا حد فى شربه لان التحريم للاحتياط وهو في الحد فى درأه ولو طبخ الخمر وغيره بعد الاشتداد حتى يذهب ثلثـــاه لم يحل لان الحزمة قد تقررت فلا ترتفع بالطبخ
And there is no hadd punishment for drinking it, because the prohibition is precautionary, and in hadd there is aversion (dira'). And if wine or other after becoming intense is cooked until two-thirds of it go away, it does not become halal, because the prohibition has become established and does not rise by cooking.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage provides two final rulings. First, for the type of nabidh discussed earlier (the one that became haram only due to precaution after adding dates), there is no hadd punishment for drinking it. The reason: the prohibition in this case is based on precaution (ihtiyat), not on certainty. Since hadd punishments are averted by doubts (shubhat), no hadd is applied. Second, the passage reaffirms a key rule: If wine (khamr) becomes intense and intoxicating, even if it is then cooked until two-thirds evaporate, it does NOT become halal. This is because the prohibition of wine is established textually (Quran and Sunnah) and is not removed by any cooking process. The term 'thuluthah' here refers to the evaporation of two-thirds, which works for grape juice ( عصير ) but not for wine that has already become khamr.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): The fixed corporal punishment for drinking alcohol (among other crimes).
خمر (khamr): Wine or any intoxicating drink made from grapes.
التحريم للاحتياط (al-tahrim li al-ihtiyat): Prohibition based on precaution rather than certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the prohibition is precautionary and hadd is averted by doubts.
C) Because the drink is actually halal.
D) Because the person did not know it was prohibited.

Key Takeaway
Hadd is averted by doubts, and the prohibition of khamr is irrevocable even by cooking.

Mixing Grape Juice and Date Infusion: Precaution Prevails
Ruling on mixing grape juice with date/raisin infusion during cooking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mixture of grape juice and date infusion, and apply the principle of precaution (ihtiyat) when rulings conflict.

Core Idea
When two substances with different cooking thresholds are mixed, the stricter ruling applies by precaution.

In this passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, the commentator clarifies the ruling for a mixture of grape juice (requiring two-thirds evaporation) and date infusion (requiring only light cooking). The Hanafi school applies the stricter rule to the mixture as a precaution against intoxication. We'll examine the reasoning, the principle of ihtiyat, and a noted scribal error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two base rulings
First, recall the individual rulings: grape juice (asir 'inab) requires two-thirds evaporation to become permissible. Date infusion (naqi' tamr) becomes permissible with any amount of cooking, even light.
2
Recognize the conflict when mixed
When grape juice and date infusion are cooked together, one substance would be halal after light cooking, the other still haram. Which ruling governs the mixture?
3
Apply the principle of precaution (ihtiyat)
The Hanafi school adopts the stricter ruling (grape juice) as a precaution against intoxication. The mixture remains haram until two-thirds have evaporated.
Precaution is a key principle in matters of intoxication.
4
Understand the exception for raisins
The commentator notes that raisins (zabib) are not analogous to grapes; the same strict rule does not apply to raisin infusion mixtures. A scribal error may have confused the text.
Raisins follow the date ruling: light cooking suffices.
5
Consider the practical application
In practice, if you mix grape juice with date infusion, you must cook until two-thirds evaporates. If you mix only dates and raisins, light cooking is sufficient.
Always identify the presence of grape-derived juice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تنبیہ یہاں مصنف نے بین العنب والتمر کے بعد فرمایا ہے۔ اوبين التمر والزبيب. حالانکہ یہ صرف انگور اور خمر کاحکم ہے نہ کہ زبیب و تمر کا اس لئے کہ منفی انگور کے حکم میں نہیں ہے تو یہ مصنف یا نا خین و ناقلین کا سہو ہے

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof by precaution (ihtiyat). The author explains two distinct cases: date infusion requires only light cooking to become permissible, while grape juice requires two-thirds evaporation. When the two are mixed, the stricter rule (grape juice) prevails due to precaution. The admonition (tanbih) notes a possible scribal error where the text mentions 'between dates and raisins' even though raisins are not analogous to grapes. The ruling is clear: mix them and you must wait for two-thirds evaporation.

Key Terms
عصیر عنب (asir 'inab): Fresh grape juice that requires two-thirds evaporation when cooked to become halal.
نقیع تمر (naqi' tamr): Date infusion (soaked dates in water) which becomes permissible with light cooking.

احتیاط (ihtiyat): Precaution; applying the stricter ruling to avoid potential prohibition.

The Principle of Preponderance in Adding Raw Fruit to Cooked Nabidh
Ruling with proof by analogy and the principle of ghalabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adding raw dates/raisins to cooked nabidh, apply the principle of ghalabah (preponderance) to mixed cases, and distinguish between negligible and significant quantities.

Core Idea
When permissibility and prohibition mix, prohibition preponderates based on quantity.

Cooked date infusion (naqi' tamr) is permissible by default. But what happens when you add fresh dates or raisins after cooking? The Hanafi school applies a nuanced rule: the quantity of the added raw fruit determines the outcome. This lesson unpacks the principle of preponderance (ghalabah) that governs such mixtures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
The act is adding raw dates or raisins to already cooked and permissible date/raisin infusion. Note that the base infusion is halal after light cooking.
2
Recognize the two scenarios based on quantity
The author distinguishes between a negligible amount (from which nabidh is not normally made) and a significant amount (from which nabidh is normally made). The ruling changes accordingly.
Negligible → remains halal; significant → entire mixture becomes haram.
3
Apply the principle of ghalabah (preponderance)
When halal and haram factors coexist, prohibition is given preponderance. Here, the added raw fruit brings a prohibition factor that overrides the permissibility of the cooked base if its quantity is substantial.
This is a rational principle (dalil aqli) used in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Examine the analogy (qiyas)
The author likens the case to pouring a cup of raw infusion into cooked nabidh: the entire mixture becomes haram. This shows that the effect of the added raw material spreads to the whole.
The analogy reinforces the principle of ghalabah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اور وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ جہاں حلت و حرمت کا اجتماع ہوتا ہے وہاں غلبہ اور ترجیح حرمت کو ہوتی ہے۔
And if date infusion and raisin infusion are cooked with a light cooking, then dates and raisins are soaked in it, if what is soaked is a small amount, from which such nabidh is not made, then there is no harm. But if nabidh is made from such a thing, it is not halal, just as when a cup of infusion is poured into cooked (nabidh). The aspect of prohibition is given preponderance. Translation: And if date infusion and raisin infusion are cooked lightly, then dates or raisins are added, if the amount added is trivial such that nabidh is not ordinarily made from the like of it, then there is no harm. But if nabidh is made from the like of it, then it is not halal, like when into cooked (nabidh) a cup of infusion is poured, and the cause of prohibition is given preponderance. Explanation: The date infusion and raisin infusion were lightly cooked, due to which they had become halal. But after cooking, dates or raisins were added again. What is the ruling? He answered: If dates or raisins are added in an amount from which nabidh is not ordinarily made—i.e., a very small amount—then it is like non-existence, and it remains halal. But if an amount is added from which nabidh is ordinarily made, then it is no longer halal but becomes forbidden. And this is exactly like when into cooked nabidh etc., which is halal, a cup of date infusion is poured, then all of it becomes forbidden. And the reason is that when permissibility and prohibition come together, preponderance and preference are given to prohibition.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof by the principle of preponderance (ghalabah). The case: cooked date and raisin infusion is already halal. If after cooking, additional dates/raisins are added, the ruling depends on quantity. If the added amount is negligible (less than what would normally be used to make nabidh), it remains halal. If it is substantial (enough to make nabidh on its own), the entire mixture becomes forbidden. This is because when halal and haram mix, the prohibition is given preponderance. The analogy given is pouring a cup of raw infusion into cooked nabidh—it all becomes haram.

Key Terms
نقیع تمر (naqi' tamr): Date infusion made by soaking dates in water.
غلبہ (ghalabah): Preponderance: when two factors conflict, the stronger one prevails. Here, prohibition preponderates over permissibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The mixture becomes forbidden
C) Only the added dates are forbidden
D) The mixture must be cooked again

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on mixtures of halal and haram depends on the quantity of the prohibited component; if significant, prohibition preponderates.

No Hadd for Precautionary Prohibition; Cooking After Ishtidad Does Not Purify
Ruling on hadd for precautionary prohibition and effect of cooking after intoxication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that no hadd is applied when prohibition is based on precaution, and explain why cooking after a drink becomes intoxicating does not purify it.

Core Idea
Hadd is averted by doubt; once prohibition is fixed by intoxication, cooking cannot remove it.

In the previous lesson, we encountered a drink that was cooked and then had date infusion added, making its status uncertain. Though it is forbidden out of precaution, the question of the hadd penalty remains. This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies two key principles: first, that hadd is not applied when the prohibition is precautionary (ihtiyat), and second, that once a drink reaches the point of strong intoxication (ishtidad), no subsequent cooking can make it halal. These rules reflect the Hanafi school’s careful approach to hudud and the permanence of prohibition after intoxication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is drinking the mixed nabidh from the previous lesson. The ruling is that drinking it is haram, but the drinker does not receive the hadd penalty.
The hadd is 80 lashes for drinking intoxicants.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is stated: 'the prohibition is for precaution, and precaution in hadd is to avert it.' This means the basis for prohibition is ihtiyat, not certainty, so the hadd is averted by doubt.
The principle: al-hadd yudra'u bi al-shubuhat (hadd is averted by doubts).
3
Derive the general rule for cooking after ishtidad
The text states that if wine or other intoxicating drinks are cooked after becoming strong (ishtidad) until two-thirds evaporate, they do not become halal. Reason: the prohibition has become established and cooking does not remove it.
This shows that cooking can only prevent prohibition from arising, not remove an existing one.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Some other schools may apply hadd if intoxication is proven, even if the prohibition is precautionary. The Hanafi school differs because it requires definitive (qat'i) proof for hadd.
This ikhtilaf stems from different standards of certainty for hudud.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred view)
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that no hadd is applied in cases of precautionary prohibition, and that cooking after ishtidad does not make the drink halal. This is the view of the majority of Hanafi jurists as recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
Al-Hidayah itself affirms this as the correct position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا حد فى شربه لان التحريم للاحتياط وهو في الحد فى درأه ولو طبخ الخمر وغيره بعد الاشتداد حتى يذهب ثلثـــاه لم يحل لان الحزمة قد تقررت فلا ترتفع بالطبخ

ترجمہ اور اس کے پینے میں حد نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ تحریم احتیاط کی وجہ سے ہے اور احتیاط حد میں حد دور کرنے میں ہے اور اگر خمر یا غیر خمر کو اشتد اد کے بعد پکا دیا گیا یہاں تک کہ اس کے دو ٹکٹ جل گئے تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ حرمت جم چکی ہے تو وہ پکانے کی وجہ سے اٹھے گی نہیں۔ تشریح شراب کی مذکورہ تم کہ جس کو پکا دیا گیا تھا لیکن بعد میں اندر اس کے نقیح تمر ڈالی گئی ہوتواس کا پینا حرام ہے کیونکہ احتیاط کا یہی تقاضہ ہے۔ لیکن اگر کسی نے پی لی تو حد کے کیا نہیں؟ تو فرمایا کہ د کے اندر احتیاط دور کرنے میں ہے لہذ احد نہیں ماری جائے گی ۔ شراب بن کر تیار ہو چکی ہے جس میں جوش و تیزی اور سکر موجود ہے خواہ خمر ہو یا غیر خمر اب اس کو پکایا جا رہا ہے تا کہ دو ثلث جل جائے تو یہ حلال نہ ہوگی ، اس لئے کہ پکانا مانع ثبوت حرمت ہے رافع ثبوت حرمت نہیں ہے۔
And there is no hadd in drinking it because the prohibition is for precaution, and precaution in hadd is to avert it. And if wine or other (drinks) after becoming strong are cooked until two-thirds go, it does not become halal because the prohibition has become established and does not rise by cooking. Translation: And there is no hadd in drinking it because the prohibition is due to precaution, and precaution in hadd is in averting it. And if wine or other than wine is cooked after it becomes strong until two-thirds of it are gone, it is not halal because the prohibition is fixed and will not be removed by cooking. Explanation: The aforementioned drink was cooked, but later date infusion was added to it, so drinking it is forbidden because precaution requires that. But if someone drank it, is hadd imposed? He said that precaution in hadd is to avert it, so hadd will not be applied. Wine has become ready in which there is ferment and intoxication, whether it is wine or other. Now it is being cooked so that two-thirds go, then it will not be halal, because cooking is a preventer of the establishment of prohibition, not a remover of established prohibition.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings: first, regarding the mixed drink from the previous lesson—even though drinking it is forbidden due to precaution, no hadd penalty is applied because the prohibition is not definitive; hadd is averted by doubt (ihtiyat fi al-hadd). Second, if wine (khamr) or any intoxicating drink that has already become strong (ishtadda) is cooked to remove two-thirds, it does not become halal. Once prohibition is established, it cannot be reversed by cooking; cooking only prevents initial prohibition from arising, it does not remove an existing one.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Fixed legal penalty for drinking intoxicants (e.g., 80 lashes).
اشتداد (ishtidad): The point where a beverage becomes strongly intoxicating (fermentation complete).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is there no hadd for drinking the mixed nabidh mentioned in the previous lesson?
A) Because it is not intoxicating
B) Because the prohibition is based on precaution, and hadd is averted by doubt
C) Because the drink is halal
D) Because the penalty has been abrogated

Key Takeaway
Hadd is only applied when the prohibition is definitive; precautionary prohibition and cooking after intoxication do not trigger the hadd penalty.

Cooking After Fermentation Does Not Make Wine Halal
Ruling on cooking intoxicating drinks after fermentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cooking intoxicating drinks after ishtidad and distinguish between cooking as a preventer vs. a remover.

Core Idea
Once a drink becomes intoxicating, cooking cannot reverse its prohibition — cooking prevents, it does not remove.

Many people mistakenly believe that boiling wine for a long time removes its impurity and makes it permissible to drink. Hanafi fiqh sharply distinguishes between the stages of fermentation. If cooking occurs before the drink becomes intensely intoxicating (ishtidad), it can prevent the prohibition from taking effect. But once the drink has fully fermented and is intoxicating, no amount of cooking can restore its permissibility. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the underlying legal principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is cooking (tabkh) a drink that has already reached ishtidad (full intoxication). The ruling is that the drink remains haram, even if two-thirds evaporate.
2
Understand the Key Moment – Ishtidad
Ishtidad is the point at which the drink becomes strongly intoxicating. Before this point, the drink is not yet prohibited (though it may become so). After this point, prohibition is established.
The Hanafi school defines khamr by its intoxicating quality, not its source material.
3
Distinguish Pre- and Post-Ishtidad Cooking
Cooking before ishtidad prevents the cause of prohibition from occurring. Cooking after ishtidad does not remove an already fixed prohibition. The distinction is rooted in the concepts of mani' (preventer) and rafi' (remover).
Cooking is a preventer (mani') of the establishment of prohibition, not a remover (rafi') of an existing prohibition.
4
Grasp the Principle – Mani' vs. Rafi'
The principle is that once a prohibition is established (by the presence of intoxication), it is legally immutable through subsequent physical changes. Cooking cannot erase the legal impurity.
This applies to both khamr (grape wine) and other intoxicating drinks (non-khamr).
5
Apply to Both Khamr and Non-Khamr Intoxicants
The ruling is general: any intoxicating drink that has reached ishtidad remains haram after cooking. There is no distinction between grape wine and other fermented beverages.
The term 'khamr' in the text includes both actual wine and other drinks that cause intoxication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو طبخ الخمر وغيره بعد الاشتداد حتى يذهب ثلثـــاه لم يحل لان الحزمة قد تقررت فلا ترتفع بالطبخ

ترجمہ اور اگر خمر یا غیر خمر کو اشتد اد کے بعد پکا دیا گیا یہاں تک کہ اس کے دو ٹکٹ جل گئے تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ حرمت جم چکی ہے تو وہ پکانے کی وجہ سے اٹھے گی نہیں۔
تشریح شراب بن کر تیار ہو چکی ہے جس میں جوش و تیزی اور سکر موجود ہے خواہ خمر ہو یا غیر خمر اب اس کو پکایا جا رہا ہے تا کہ دو ثلث جل جائے تو یہ حلال نہ ہوگی ، اس لئے کہ پکانا مانع ثبوت حرمت ہے رافع ثبوت حرمت نہیں ہے۔
And if wine or other (drinks) are cooked after they become strong until two-thirds go, it does not become halal because the prohibition has become established and does not rise by cooking. Translation: And if wine or other than wine is cooked after it becomes strong until two-thirds of it are gone, it is not halal because the prohibition is fixed and will not be removed by cooking. Explanation: The wine has become ready, in which there is ferment and intoxication, whether it is wine or other. Now it is being cooked so that two-thirds go; then it will not be halal, because cooking prevents the establishment of prohibition, it does not remove established prohibition.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is a ruling with a clear distinction: cooking (tabkh) can prevent a drink from becoming prohibited if done before fermentation (ishtidad). But once the drink has become intoxicating (ishtadda), cooking cannot reverse the prohibition. The analogy: cooking prevents the cause of prohibition from taking effect; it does not eliminate an existing prohibition. This applies to both khamr (grape wine) and other intoxicating drinks (non-khamr). The key principle: 'cooking is a preventer (mani') of the establishment of prohibition, not a remover (rafi') of established prohibition.'

Key Terms
اشتداد (ishtidad): The moment when the drink becomes strongly intoxicating (fermentation complete).
مانع ثبوت حرمت (mani' thubut al-hurmah): A factor that prevents the establishment of prohibition. Cooking before ishtidad is such a preventer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can wine (khamr) that has already become intoxicating be made halal by cooking until two-thirds evaporate?
A) Yes, because the alcohol is removed
B) No, because the prohibition is already established and cooking does not remove it
C) Yes, if it is cooked for a long time
D) No, unless water is added

Key Takeaway
Cooking after a drink becomes intoxicating does not make it halal; prohibition is established and cannot be removed by cooking.

Cooking Intoxicating Drinks Does Not Make Them Halal
Ruling on cooking wine after it becomes intoxicating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on drinking an intoxicating beverage that has been cooked after becoming strong, and explain why the hadd penalty is not applied.

Core Idea
Once prohibition is established, cooking cannot reverse it; hadd is suspended by caution.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding the effect of cooking on an already prohibited intoxicating drink. The passage clarifies that neither burning two-thirds of the liquid nor adding other ingredients after cooking can make the drink halal. It also explains why the fixed penalty (hadd) is not applied if someone consumes it — a reflection of the Hanafi principle of ihtiyat (caution) in hudud cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
What is the hukm (ruling) on drinking an intoxicating beverage that was cooked until two-thirds evaporated? The passage states it remains haram.
The prohibition is already 'established' (جم چکی) and cooking is not a remover.
2
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
Why does cooking not remove the prohibition? Because the cause of prohibition (intoxication) existed and the ruling became fixed. Cooking does not address the original cause; it only changes the physical state.
This is a ta'lil (reasoning) based on the principle that an established ruling cannot be reversed by a subsequent event that does not negate the cause.
3
Analyze the penalty (hadd) aspect
The passage states that if someone drinks it, no hadd is applied. Why? Because the prohibition was established out of caution (ihtiyat), and caution in hudud means suspending punishment in doubtful cases.
The absence of hadd does not imply the act is halal; it reflects procedural caution.
4
Apply to a variant scenario
Consider the case where date juice (نقیع تمر) is added after cooking. The ruling remains haram because the prohibition is already fixed.
Adding new ingredients does not remove the established prohibition.
5
Summarize the legal principle
The key takeaway: cooking can prevent prohibition from arising (مانع ثبوت), but it cannot remove prohibition once established (رافع ثبوت).
This principle is general for all intoxicants (khamr and non-khamr) once they become intoxicating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ اور اس کے پینے میں حد نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ تحریم احتیاط کی وجہ سے ہے اور احتیاط حد میں حد دور کرنے میں ہے اور اگر خمر یا غیر خمر کو اشتداد کے بعد پکا دیا گیا یہاں تک کہ اس کے دو ثلث جل گئے تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ حرمت جم چکی ہے تو وہ پکانے کی وجہ سے اٹھے گی نہیں۔ تشریح شراب کی مذکورہ تم کہ جس کو پکا دیا گیا تھا لیکن بعد میں اندر اس کے نقیع تمر ڈالی گئی ہو تو اس کا پینا حرام ہے کیونکہ احتیاط کا یہی تقاضہ ہے۔ لیکن اگر کسی نے پی لی تو حد کے کیا نہیں؟ تو فرمایا کہ در کے اندر احتیاط دور کرنے میں ہے لہذا حد نہیں ماری جائے گی ۔ شراب بن کر تیار ہو چکی ہے جس میں جوش و تیزی اور سکر موجود ہے خواہ خمر ہو یا غیر خمر اب اس کو پکایا جا رہا ہے تا کہ دو ثلث جل جائے تو یہ حلال نہ ہوگی ، اس لئے کہ پکانا مانع ثبوت حرمت ہے رافع ثبوت حرمت نہیں ہے۔
Translation: And there is no fixed hadd penalty for drinking it because the prohibition is out of caution, and caution lies in removing the hadd. And if khamr or other than khamr is cooked after it has become strong until two-thirds of it are burnt, then it is not halal because the prohibition has been established, and it will not be removed by cooking. Explanation: The mentioned wine that was cooked but later date juice was added to it, drinking it is haram because that is what caution requires. But if someone drank it, is the hadd penalty not applied? He said that caution lies in removing the hadd, so the hadd will not be applied. Once wine is ready with effervescence and intoxication, whether khamr or non-khamr, if it is cooked so that two-thirds burn, it will not be halal, because cooking prevents the establishment of prohibition, it does not remove established prohibition.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is a ruling_statement from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addressing the effect of cooking on already-prohibited intoxicating drinks. The passage clarifies that once a drink becomes intoxicating and thus haram, subsequent cooking (even burning two-thirds) does not render it halal. The prohibition is already 'established' (جم چکی) and cooking is only a preventive measure (مانع ثبوت) not a remover (رافع) of established prohibition. An interesting detail: if someone drinks such cooked wine, the hadd penalty is not applied due to caution (احتیاط) — meaning the legal penalty is suspended in doubtful cases. The commentary also addresses a scenario where date juice (نقیع تمر) is added after cooking; it remains haram. No explicit proof from Quran or Sunnah is cited here; the reasoning is based on the principle that an established ruling cannot be reversed by a later action that does not address the original cause of prohibition.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine made from grapes; in this context, any intoxicating drink (including non-grape) that has become prohibited.
حد (hadd): The fixed corporal penalty for consuming intoxicants (generally 80 lashes for free men).
تحریم (tahrim): The act of rendering something haram; here, the state of being prohibited.
احتیاط (ihtiyat): Legal caution; used here to justify not applying hadd in doubtful cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on drinking an intoxicating beverage that was cooked after it became strong, burning two-thirds of it?
A) It becomes halal because the intoxicant is removed.
B) It remains haram because the prohibition was already established.
C) It is makruh but not haram.
D) It is halal only if no other substance is added.

Key Takeaway
Once a drink becomes intoxicating and thus haram, subsequent cooking — even to the point of burning two-thirds — does not make it halal; the prohibition is fixed, and the hadd penalty is suspended due to caution, not because the act becomes permissible.
